In addition to those named the register

Mr. Van Wyck confessed that the

ial candidates had been done by a pre

Lew Byinrton and the others that you
have mentioned to me. It was sug

It was not of the committee, for it was

Mr. Putnam was not one of those who
was colonized. The place that he
moved to, 1020 Washington street, he
was born in, and lived there up to four
years ago when he was married. Last
May his stepmother died, and his
father, who is an old man of 70, asked
his boy to come and live with him in
his later years, and on August 1, before
Mr. Putnam dreamed of running for
Supervisor, he moved there."
Here is the direct admission of one of
the members of the committee which
made the nominate- that a coloniza
tion scheme was suggested to the com
mittee and carried out. Every infer
ence given in this interview is that this

It is a plain statement that

Seventh, Ninth or Tenth
wards who was fit to associ

The men who were colonized by Louis

These candidates who were colonized
by Mayor Phelan were seen yesterday

While Isadore Gutte is ostensibly a

almost any time out of business hours.
Commodore Gutte was bubbling over
with good nature when asked about his

the Leavenworth street place, although
I've taken rooms at the Occidental," he

cidental before I was nominated for
Supervisor, and I don't think you can
make much political capital out of it,"
and the Jolly commodore chuckled at
something, possibly at the same thing

At the Byington home, 1412 Post

Byington lived there; that he came

Lewis Byington had a room there; that

Jeremiah Deasy, with hia brother,
has a shoe store at 20 Sixth street. His
family resides at 834 Fulton street,
where they have lived for several years.
Mr. Deasy is essentially a home man,
who cares more for the pleasures and
comforts of his fireside than for all the
political emoluments a grateful con
stituency could bestow, yet Mr. Deasy,
at the "suggestion" of Mayor Phelan,

He has not entirely forsaken his family

cept of their invitation to stay all
night, leaving room 11 In the Sixth

nonce. Mr. Deasy frankly admitted
that he had changed his residence for
the sole purpose of becoming eligible as
a candidate from the Tenth Ward, and
said that after the Bth of November,
whether he was elected or not, he would
return to his Fulton street home.
"I took up my residence on Sixth
street," said Mr. Deasy, "at the sug
gestion of my lawyers, and on their as
surance that it was perfectly legal and
proper. I undoubtedly shall return to
my Fulton-street home after the elec
tion. I don't see that there is anything
wrong about that. I formerly lived in
the Tenth Ward, and I know 800 men
in that ward to every one I know In
the Twelfth."
Mayor Phelan's ideas on the subject
of colonization of citizens for political
purposes have undergone a change since
he so emphatically denounced the com
mutation of the sentence of Louis
Sternberg. Then it was a crime â no
less a crime than treason â and should
be punished by imprisonment for life
without the power of pardon. Now it is
a laughing matter, and should be done
in the interests of "purity in politics."
"It has been known all along that
candidates for places on the Board of
Supervisors had moved into their wards
in which they intended to run in order
to qualify themselves for election," said
Mr. Phelan last evening when asked to
explain his ideas on the subject of such
colonization. "When the committee on
nominations for Supervisors made its
report to the convention there was a
statement included which informed the
convention that where suitable men
could not be found In a ward others
had been asked to qualify themselves
by establishing a residence In the ward
from which they were nominated. They
had time to do that and they did so.
"The idea was that at the last Legis
lature a law was gassed abolishing the
wards of San Francisco, but this lav/
is considered by many to be unconsti

for San Francisco. To avoid any com
plication therefore it was deemed ad
visable to have the candidates who did
not live in the wards for which they
were nominated establish a residence
there at once.
"I was not consulted on this matter,
but had they consulted me I certainly
would have advised the course taken.
I would have done it myself had I been
in their position."
Here Is a plain statement from the
man who poses as the purifier of poli

that he would have colonized himself

laughed" when he read in The Call that
he was charged with inducing citizens
to change their residences for political

Of course he laughed. It was his own
Joke. And when Phelan laughs it is in
cumbent upon his Subsidized Bulletin to
laugh. Not exactly a "horse laugh,"
but one from another kind of animal of
the equine species. Something in the
nature of a bray. This combination of
Phelan's laugh and the Subsidized Bul
letin's bray rings badly in the ears of
the voters who will be the ones to de
cide the question of who shall look after

8. The laugh and the bray are not in
harmony with the fierce denunciation of
the deed of Sternberg, and the people
are wondering that desire for office on
the part of one man and greed for more
subsidizing on the part of a paper
should so change the ideas of right and
wrong in the short space of a year.

PROUDLY WAVES
OVER SAN JUAN
Crowds Cheer and All Heads Are
Bared as the Stars and
Stripes Are Unfurled.
Porto Rico Being Formally in Uncle Sard's Pos
session the Labors of t\)z Evacuation
Coromission End.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 18.â The United States has been
formally placed in sovereign possession of the island of
Porto Rico. The War Department has received the fol
lowing dispatch:
"SAN JUAN, Porto Rico, Oct. 18.â Secretary of War,
Washington: Our flags have been raise*d on the public
buildings and forts in this city and saluted with national
salutes. The occupation of the island is now complete.
"BROOKE."
A copy of General Brooke's dispatch was sent to the
President at Chicago immediately upon its receipt.
SAN JUAN DE PORTO RICO, Oct. i
18.â Promptly at noon to-day the Amer
ican flag was raised over San Juan.
The ceremony was quiet and dignified,
unmarred by disorder of any kind.
The Eleventh Regiment Infantry,
with two batteries of the Fifth Artil
lery, landed this morning. The latter
proceeded to the forts, while the in
fantry lined up on the docks. It was
a hot day for San Juan, and there were
many people in the streets. Rear Ad
miral Schley and General Gordon, ac
companied by their staffs, proceeded to
the palace in carriages. The Eleventh
Infantry Regiment and band, with
Troop H of the Sixth United States
Cavalry, then marched through the
streets and formed in the square oppo
site the palace.
At 11:40 a. m. General Brooke, Ad
miral Schley and General Gordon, the
United States Evacuation Commission
ers, came out of the palace with many
naval officers and formed on the right
side of the square.
The streets behind the soldiers were
. thronged with townspeople, who stood
waiting in dead silence. At last the
city clock struck the hour of 12, and
the crowds, almost breathless and with
eyes fixed upon the flagpole, watched
for developments. At the sound of the
first gun from Fort Mnrro, Major Dean
and Lieutenant Castle of General
Brooke's staff hoisted the stars and
stripes, while the band played the
"Star Spangled Banner."
All heads were bared and the crowds
cheered. Fort Morro, Fort San Cris
tobal and the United States revenue
cutter Manning, lying in the harbor,
fired twenty-one guns each. Senor
Munox Rivera, who was president of
the recent autonomist council of secre
taries, and other officials of the late in
sular government were present at the
proceedings.
Congratulations and handshaking
among the American officers followed.
Ensign King hoisted the stars and
stripes on the Intendencia, but all other
flags on the various public buildings
were hoisted by military officers. Si
multaneously with the raising of the
flag over the Captain General's palace
many others were hoisted in different
parts of the city.
The work of the United States Evac
uation Commission is now over, and all
the reports will be forwarded to "Wash^
ington on Thursday next. The labors
of both parties have terminated with
honors for all concerned. The Ameri
can Commissioners worked without the
least delay, and in the most thorough
and effective manner.

CITIZENSHIP OF
THE PORTO RICANS
WASHINGTON, Oct. 18.â In view of
the fact that at noon to-day, with the
acquisition of the island of Porto Rico
by the United States, about 800,000 in
habitants of that island lost their
Spanish citizenship, the question as to
what shall become Â»of these people po
litically becomes of importance.
Madrid advices report that leading
citizens in Porto Rico have already ta
ken steps to become naturalized as
United States citizens. This is an er
ror, for existing laws provide no way
in which the change of citizenship can
be effected by the residents of the isl
and. There is no United States Court
there before which a declaration of in
tention can be filed, ' and, in fact, no
machinery at all which can be put in
motion to change citizenship.
Inquiry into this subject at the State
Department discloses the fact that it
has been usual to provide a bodily
transfer of the citizenship of people
conditioned as those in Porto Rico in
the treaty of peace which terminates a
war. It may be prudent for the com
mission at Paris to avoid any reference
to Porto Rico again, in which case
Congress will be called upon by the
President to confer citizenship upon
the Porto Ricans in the same act as
that which must be passed to nrovide a
stable form of government for the isl
and. Should either of these courses
be found impracticable, however, it is
said at the department that interna
tional law in its operation would con
fer American citizenship upon these
Porto Ricans. the general principle be
ing that where a treaty of cession is
silent upon the subject citizenship
changes with the soil. In the case of
the acquisition of New Mexico, the res
idents of the Territory were given
American citizenship by specific provi
sion of the treaty.

TWO JEN LOST
IN CROSSING
THE GLACIER
Fortune Hunters Die
Miserably.
COULD NOT BE RESCUED
DNE DISAPPEARED, A SECOND
LEFT CALLING FOR AID.
One Hundred and Eighty Gold Hunt
ers Bound for Lake Atlin Are
Also in Danger of Per
ishing.
BY HAL HOFFMAN.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
JUNJEAU, Alaska, Oct. 14 (by steam
ship Dirigo to Victoria, B. C, Oct. 18).â
Considerable gold is being brought out
of the new diggings at Lake Atlin by
*he latest outcomers, arriving here and
at Skaguay to spend the winter and
make arrangements to return early in
the spring. An occasional nugget is
exhibited, but they are scarcer than
are found in the Dawson mines. It is
mostly coarse gold from the new dig
gings, clean and bright. L. McKinnon,
one of the first locators near Discov
ery, and Dr. Lovett and Mr. Wheeler
arrived here this morning. McKinnon
made a neat clean-up on a quantity of
provisions he took in by pack train
<over the Taku trail.
A line of small steamboats will be
put on the Taku Inlet run from Juneau
next spring. Enough men are going
In from the coast towns of Alaska to
make a camp of three or four thou
sand people soon after the ice breaks
lip In the rivers, and there will be con
siderable movement of supplies to At-
Un City over the snow this winter. No
-new strikes have been made, but all
reports continue favorable.
The caravan of 180 men with horses,
who struck across the country for At
lin from Teslin City, the Stickeen River
trail and Glenora has not been heard
from or seen since it started out. They
eet out to follow a large valley run
ning across country, in which there was
said to be grass for the horses. Sev
eral parties have arrived out from Lake
-Teslin by way of the Taku trail. They
learned of the departure of the cara
van, but saw nothing of them. The
attempt was a dangerous one with win
ter so close at hand, and it is feared
that the entire expedition will perish.
That will certainly be the result if the
party gets snowed in, as tney were not
burdened with provisions. On account !
of the lack of horse feed they were able
to purchase the horses for as low a
price as $1 each.
A party of eighty Klondikers came
Up the river on the little steamer Clara
and arrived at Skaguay in time to
catch the Dirigo. Some of them re
port an increasing interest in Pine
Creek and Lake Atlin country. A
number left the party when they ar
rived at Lake Marsh and went over to
Atlin by way of Tuku Arm to take a
look at the country. The Klondikers
bring out about $60,000 in dust, and an
unknown amount of checks and drafts.
Most of the party on the Clara are
men who went to Dawson in the rush
last winter and spring, locating noth
ing of value and are returning home
empty handed.
The death rate was sti!l very high
ten days ago. but winter is expected j
to kill the typhoid epidemic soon. Daw
sonites are interested in suppressing
news of the large amount of deaths,
and for this and other reasons it is
probable that the whole number will
never be known.' The steamship Ex.
celsior was towed up here last night
from Valdes and other ports to th% i
westward. She brought down about !
100 Copper River argonauts, who tell |
the same hard luck story of their exÂ»
perience in the Copper River country-*
no gold, except a coior now and then.
This is the last batch of Copper River
gold seekers to come out this season.
About 400 people in all will winter In
the country and next summer go up th&
river and over the divide down into thÂ»
Tanona and White River country. They
are camping at the head and foot of
Klutena Lake, at the hÂ«ad of the can
yon, and a few at Valdea.
Captain Abercrombie of the Govern
ment military expedition has about a
dozen men on his hands at Valdes who
have neither money nor food. He will
send them to Pupet Sound or San Fran
cisco on the first Government vessel.
One of the latest parties to com* j
over the glacier, lost two men in crev- ;
lc>"*s. Their names arÂ» not known. Ons j
man was never heard from after h*
broke through the snow crust and dis
appeared. The other man was heard
calling for help down in the black
chasm. Everything possible was donÂ«
to rescue him with the means at hand, j
A rope with a rock tied to the end was j
lowered, but he could not reach It,

STEAMER BRIXHAM WRECKED
ON A BLASHE ISLAND REEF
STEAMER BRIXHAM, WRECKED ON BLASHE ISLAND.
SEATTLE, Wash., Oct. 18.â The steamer Brixham is the latest vessel wrecked in the treacherous waters of
the North. She struck on a reef early las? Thursday morning a quarter of a mile off Blashe Island, near
the head of Clarence Strait, and thirty miles south of Wrangel. All the passengers and crew were res
cued. Officers of the steamer Dirigo, just In port from Lynn Canal, report that the Brixham is lying
on the rocks on an even keel, with her bow stove In and her hold full of water. Her cargo Is be
lieved to be destroyed. Hopes are entertained that the steamer can be saved unless southeast gales set in.
Wednesday night, prior to the disaster, was exceptionally dark, and the lowering clouds shut off the
vision of anything save a big black mantle of gloom. The nassengers and those of the crew who were off
watch were below asleep and all unconscious of impending danger, when suddenly a cannbn-like crash aroused
them. The steamer struck the hidden reef and her engines were almost shaken to pieces as they swung her
shaft around in an endeavor to drive her forward, notwithstanding the barrier of jagged rock which cut
deeper into her prow with every revolution of her propeller.
An examination of the steamer was made by the officers and It was found that her hold had filled with
water. The passengers were Immediately put ashore without the slightest accident. A camp was at once
formed. The day after the steamer Al-Kl hove in sight, and in answer to the signals of distress came along
side and at the request of the Brlxham's captain took a number of the wrecked wayfarers into Skaguay.
When the Dirigo was bound down she hove to some distance from the wreck, which then appeared to be
well filled with water and hard and fast on the reef, and a boat put out to her from the camp with a num
ber of the passengers and crew who asked a passage downward for the wrecked men. As, however, the
Dirigo was already crowded to her limit â she had 190 passengers â the request was necessarily without avail.
The reef on which the Brixham lies Is four miles off the regular course to Wrangel.
The Brixham was built in England in 1885. She was wrecked about three years ago on the Atlantic
Coast and was purchased and repaired by American parties and put under the Stars and Stripes. She was
183 feet long, 27:1 feet broad and 14:2 feet deep. She was owned by the Boston- Alaska Transportation Com
pany and came here from Baltimore to go into the Klondike trade. There is $50,000 insurance in Coast offices
on her. Her purchase price was $75,000.

HOPE FOR EASIER TERMS
"MANANA" POLICY OF SPAIN IS
THE LAST RESORT.
It Is Now Definitely Stated That the
Question of the Disposition of
the Philippines Has Not
Been Taken Up.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Oct. 18.â The Washing
ton correspondent of the Herald tele
graphs: Believing the forthcoming
elections in the United States, and es
pecially in New York, will show the
American people are opposed to the ac
quisition of onatlying territory, and that
President McKiniey will consequently
modify his demands with respect to the
Philippines, the Spanish commissioners
in Paris are exerting all their efforts to
delay action on the various points at
issue until the middle of November.
Information Bent to Washington by
Judge Day, chairman of the American
commission, has convinced the offi
cials that this Is the object of JLhe
"manana" policy now being pursued by
Senor Montero Ricrs and his associates.
Mr. Day lias peremptorily informed the
commissioners that the United States
expects negotiations to be concluded
with the utmost dispatch. It is not
meant by this that progress has not
been made. As a matter of fact the
negotiators have agreed upon several
points, and such action is very encour
aging to the officials here. But Senor
Montero Rios and his colleagues appar
ently do not understand or do not want
to understand that matters settled by
the protocol are not open to discussion.
It can be stated on authority that the
question of control, disposition and gov
ernment of the Philippines has not yet
been taken up by the commission. When
the commiaÃion held its first session the
programme to be adapted for discussion
of various questions was discussed, and
the Spanish commissioners attempted
to ascertain the intentions of the Amer
ican Government with respect to East
ern islands. Upon Judge Day's repre
sentation that it was the desire of the
American commissioners that the first
question to be disposed of should be
relating to the Independence of Cuba
and the cession of Porto Rico an/i Guam
Island to the United States the Spanish
commissioners acquiesced, but have
since been endeavoring to draw out
Judge Day on the subject of the Philip
pines. But Judge Day has been ex
tremely cautious and it is not believed
here that he yesterday informed the
Spanish commissioners that the sur
render of the Philippines would prob
ably "be demanded irrespective of the
Cuban or any other debt.

THE SAN FK AN CISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 19, 183 a.
COMMISSIONS
CANNOT AGREE
Americans and Span
iards Far Apart.
AS TO CUBAN EVACUATION
SO BUTLER AND PARRADO WILL
PRIVATELY CONFER.
However, Uncle Sam's Representa
tives Are Not Yet Ready to
Charge the Dons With
Double Dealing.
Special cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, I&SS,' by James Gor
don Bennett.
HAVANA, Oct. 18.â The two commis
sions are to-day as far as ever from an
agreement concerning the date of the
final evacuation of Cuba. Last week the
Spanish Commissioners announced that
it would be impossible to withdraw all
the troops before January 15, which
date It was then believed, would be
agreed upon.
Misunderstandings arose, and now
the commissions are so far apart that
an agreement between them seems un
likely. Colonel Glrauta to-day told me
he thought the 'matter would be re
ferred back to the Governments in
Washington and Madrid.
The Americans will make a final at
tempt to reach an understanding by
arranging an informal conference be
tween General Butler and General Par
rado. These two will meet . with no
one present save two Interpreters, and
many subjects will be discussed with
more freedom than could be permitted
In formal session.
It would seem unfair to charge the
Spaniards with double dealing, and the
Americans place no confidence in. the
reports from Paris that Premier Bn
gasta has ordered General Blanco not
to evacuate Cuba until the Philippine
question is settled. The Americans
point to the complete plan of Cuba's de
fenses placed in' their hands by General
Blanco, as cabled yesterday, as evi
dence of honorable intentions.
More than that the Spanish troops
are evacuating the island as rapidly as
possible considering the ships at their
command. The Americans flrst as
sumed control in Santiago, and since
then evacuation has been going on from
east to west. Havana will be the last
point under Spanish control.

LOS ANGELES, Oct. 18.â The new poker

The question of the validity of the ordi
nance was brought before the â¢ court
through a petition for a writ of habeas
corpus directing Chief of Police Glass to
set at liberty one Wilson who, the peti
tion recited, was being unlawfully re
strained of his freedom under the opera
tion of the new ordinance, which aimed
to suppress the vice of gambling as prac
ticed In so-called club rooms throughout
the- business sections of the city.

EMPEROR IS
GREETED BY
THE SULTAN
Royal Welcome at
Constantinople.
TURKISH JOY UNBOUNDED
ABDUL HAMID AND HIS SUB-
JECTS AT THEIR BEST.
After the Exchange of Imposing
Courtesies Magnificent Presents
Are Given the Visiting
Sovereigns.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CONSTANTINOPLE, Oct. 18.â At 8:30
a. m. the German imperial yacht Ho
henzollern, accompanied by the Ger
man warship Hela and the Sultan's
yacht Izzedin with the Turkish Imperial
Commission on board, and followed by
a number of special steamers chartered
by members of the German colony or
conveying sightseers, appeared off Old
Seraglio- Point, and the guns of the bar
racks thundered, a salute. The Hela fol
lowed with a return salute and the
Turkish b-tteries ashore and tha
guardship off Tophaney promptly re
sponded.
The Hohenzollern anchored at 8:45 a.
m. off Dolmabagtche Palace, with the
Heretha and Helva above and below
her. Soon afterward the Emperor and
Empress entered the Hohenzollern's
longboat and were rowed to the palace
landing station, where they were re
ceived by the Sultan, who was sur
rounded by a brilliant suite.
The meeting between the Sultan and
his guests was most cordial. The Sul
tan warmly shook hands with the Em
peror and Empress and, offering his
arm to the Empress, led the way to a
pavilion off the palace, where the party
had a brief rest.
Emperor 'William engaged in an ani
mated conversation with the Sultan, the
Grand Vizier and other members of the
Sultan's suite.
The route from the Dolmabagtche
Palace to the Tildiz Palace was cov
ered with red carpeting and lined with
troopÃ in new uniforms, prominent
among them being the Sultan's body
guard and the imperial body guard, in
new white uniforms embroidered with
black, white helmet, and having a
waistband of Germany's colors. The
whole made a magnificent display.
Standing: a little apart from the crowd
were large gatherings of Turkish
women who ocupled a slope facing the
Dolmabagtche Palace. In the rush of
the people to secure noints of vantaire
they climbed trees, railings and posts.
The Sultan has presented to Emperor
William a magnificent sword and to the
Empress a diamond necklace of almost
priceless value. He has also authorized
a German syndicate to construct a com
mercial post at Haidar, opposite Con
stantinople, a concession long sought
by German promoters.

GENEROUS
GIFTS TO THE
UNIVERSITY
Mrs. Hearst and Oth
ers Contribute.
ELEVEN PRIZES FOX PLANS
FINAL JUDGMENT TO BE GIVEN
IN THIS CITY.
Regent Reinstein Tells of the Pro
posed Enlargement of the Berk
eley Institution and of the
Progress of the Work.
Special cabls to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1898, by James Gor
don Bennett.
PARIS, Oct. 18.â In the Paris edition
of the Herald on Sunday was published
a communication giving the result of
the University of California architect
ural competition held at Antwerp.
Eleven competitors received prizes. The
plans will come up for final judgment
before an international jury which
meets in San Francisco.
Mrs. Hearst is at present In Paris
and the correspondent of the Herald
saw her on Monday In the hope of ob
taining some idea of the scope of the
plans and cost. J. B. Reinstein, regent
of the University of California, who
represented the institution at the Ant
werp competition, presented me to Mrs.
Hearst and explained my errand.
"You really must not ask me about
anything," exclaimed Mrs. Hearst. "I
do not want to talk about the matter.
Mr. Reinstein will tell you everything."
"But Mrs. Hearst," I said, "will you
not give me in a few words an idea
of what you propose to do and an es
timate of the cost?"
"Ask Mr. Reinstein; he will tell you
everything," were Mrs. Hearst's last
words.
"Mrs. Hearst always refuses to be in
terviewed upon what she does or hopes
to do," said Mr. Reinstein. "She is ex
ceedingly reticent in regard to her gift
to the University of California,
"To B. R. .Maybeck, instructor of
architectural drawing, belongs the
credit of originating the scheme. Then
Mrs. Hearst came forward and made it
Eosslble for the idea to be developed,
he has selected as trustees to carry
out her wishes Governor James H.
Budd of California, Professor William
Carey Jones of the faculty and myself.
Mr. Maybeck was granted a year's
leave of absence. He and I visited all
the principal cities of the United States
seeking information rrom the best
known men in educational circles. A
meeting of architects and artists was
held in New York at which tne project
was discussed and at which about two
hundred and fifty gentlemen were pres
ent. Mr. Maybeck then came to Eu
rope, visited all the great seats of
learning in the British empire and on
the Continent. I joined him here and
then we returned to San Francisco and
arrangements were made for the com
petition which has just been held at
Antwerp."
"Will Mrs. Hearst give all the money
needed to carry out the plan?" I asked.
"Oh, no. While Mrs. Hearst is a great
friend of the university there are others
who intend to assist at this work."
"About how much money will be re
quired?"
"It is impossible to state any amount.
I can say that the State of California
has set aside $1,000,000 for the buildings.
Generous friends of the university have
promised the funds necessary to erect
several other buildings and Mrs. Hearst
in addition to Tarnishing the money for
the new buildings for schools of mines
and for the endowment of its faculty
will present the institution with one or
more additional buildings."

is likely to occur soon. It is not proba
ble that he will be court-martialed.

THEY SLOGGED AT WILL.
California Athletic Club's Monthly
Entertainment in Franklin Hall.
The California Athletic and Swimming
Club gave an athletic show last evening
in Franklin Hall. The event of the
evening was an eight-round contest for
the alleged amateur lightweight cham
pionship of the coast (the rules of the j
American Athletic Association of tne
A. A. A. have made boxing professional in I
all events) between Charles Rochette and
Kid O'Brien. Jack Stelzner was selected
to referee the bout, and, being unable to
decide which of the boxers was entitled
to a decision at the end of the eighth
round, he ordered another round. Ro
chette, evidently angerod because Stelzner
did not favor, him with a decision, went
right at his game in hammer-and-tongs |
style, and O'Brien was receiving such a j
heavy bombardment of rights and lefts \
that Sergeant Anderson, anticipating a j
slaughter, stepped into the ring and or
dered the contest stopped, which mandate I
was obeyed. The referee then awarded
the fight to Rochette. *^ V
The preliminaries consisted of . a four
round . bout between Frank Ely and M.
Meyer, which was as uninteresting as
could possibly be, and a six-round con
test between Frank McGinnis and Emll
Dahl. This terminated in the third round
â¢by a knockout, McGinnis being the man
who was hard hit.. .â - Â- , .

' MARKET ST. SfVSSS^ ,"WELCOME TO
AMERICA, SWEET,
BELOVED PEACE!"
Formal Opening of Chicago's Great
Jubilee With Impressive
Ceremonies.
President McKinley, Archbishop Irelarjd and
Others Eloquently Teli of tbe War in
Bebalf of Huroanity.
â¢CHICAGO, Oct. IS.â Five thousand !
people packed within the walls of the
Auditorium witnessed the formal
launching of the peace jubilee to-day.
It required the uncorking of six viala
ot eloquence to make the launching a
distinct success, and no vessel that ever !
slid down the ways was dumped Into
moretdampness than the peace Jubilee.
A heavy drizzle that changed into rain
and then back into drizzle was falling,
the streets were filled with puddles of
water and the cold wind blowing off
Lake Michigan made walking unpleas
ant. Notwithstanding these obstacles
the opening of the jubilee proper was a
distinct and brilliant success.
It was announced that the ceremo
ftiles would commepce at 10:30 a. m.,
and fully an hour prior to that time
the street in front of the Auditorium
;was packed with an enthusiastic crowd.
'After the doors had been thrown open
there was a long wait for the speak
ers. Owing to a misunderstanding, in
â¢which the President had no part, it
was past 11 a. m. before the President
entered the hall. Upon his entrance
he was escorted by the Chicago Hus
ears and mounted police. As the Pres
ident entered the hall his appearance
was the signal for an outburst of en
thusiastic cheers that lasted for several
minutes. With the President were ex-
Governor Richard J. Oglesby of Illi
nois, Lafayette McWilliams, President
Harper of the Chicago University and
Thomas B. Bryan of Chicago. The
party composing Mrs. and Miss Mew 11
fiams and Miss Duncan were in the box
adjoining that of the President In
the first box at the left of the stage
was General Miles, and in the boxes
next to him were the members of the
various diplomatic corps. Secretary
Gage was in a box in the second tier
almost directly opposite to the box of
the President. Almost immediately af
ter the narllamentary party had taken
their seats, the Rev. Frank W. Gunsau
lus, chaplain of the meeting, offered
Chairman Charles Truax, on behalf
of the Jubilee committee, then made a
Bhort opening address and introduced
George R. Peck as presiding officer.
Mr. Peck made a brief address, and
Introduced Mayor Harrison, who deliv
ered the formal address of welcome to
President McKinley and the strangers
who had come to Chicago to witness
the ceremonies of jubilee week. The
President, who received a most enthu
siastic welcome as he entered the
building, made no formal reply to the
addresses of welcome, notwithstanding
loud calls for a speech. Following thf
ad'lrpps of Mayor Harrison, Archbishop
Ireland of St. Paul spoke.
In concluding his address Archbishop
Ireland said:
"Welcome to America, sweet, beloved
peac**; Welcome to America, honored,
elnrinus victory! Oh. peace, thou art
heaven's gift to men! When the Savior of
humanity was horn in Bethlehem, the
sang forth "Glory to God in the
highest, and on earth peace. good will to
men." P-raee was offered to the world
thr-i'iph Christ, and when the spirit of
Christ )s supreme there Is universal peace
âpeace among men, peace among na
tions. ,
O. peace, so precious art thou to hu
manity that our highest ideal of social
felicity must ever be thy sovereignty upon
earth. Pagan statesmanship, speaking
through pagan poetry, exclaims. "The
best of things which it is given to men
to know la peace; better than a thousand
triumphs is th" simple gift of peace.
The regenerated world shall not lift
Bword against sword, neither shall they
be exercised any more In war."
Peace is the moral flow of humanity's
life, the healthy pulsation of humanity's
social organism, the vital condition for
humanity's growth and happiness. Re
duce to the smallest the sacrifice on the
battlefield, one has died and some one
has hppn bereft. "Only one killed." the

chief IS lost sight of; the stomach is the
origin to be l<.<>k.-d after.
Nervous dyspeptics often do not have
any pain whatever in the stomach, nor
perhaps any of the usual symptoms of
stomach weakness. Nervous dyspep-
sia shows itself not in the stomach so
much as in nearly every other organ;
In some cases the heart palpitates and
Is irregular; in others the kidneys are
affected; in others the bowels are con-
stipated, with headache; still others
are troubled with loss of flesh and ap-
petite, with the accumulation of gas,
gour risings and heartburn.

Special Dispatch to TbÂ« Call.
headline reads. The glad news speeds.
The newsboys cry "Killed only one!"
"He was my son! what were a thousand
to this one â my only son?"
It was Wellington who said, "Take my
word for it, if you had seen but one day
of war you would pray to Almighty God
that you misht never see such a thing
again." It was Napoleon who said, "The
sipht of a battletlfld after the fight is
enough to inspire princes with a love of
peace and a horror of war."
War, be thou gone from my soul's
sight! I thank the good God that thy
ghastly specter stands no longer upon
the thresholds of the home of my fellow
countrymen in America or of my fellow
beings in distant Andalusia. When, I ask
heaven, shall humanity rise to such
heights of reason and of religion that war
shall be Impossible and stories of battle
fields but the saddening echoes of primi
tive ages of the race?
Yet a Just and necessary war is holy;
the men who at their country"s call en
gugf In such a war are the country's
heroes, to whom must be given unstinted
gratitude and unstinted praise. The sword
in their hands is the emblem of self
sarrince and of valor; the flag which
leads them betokens their country and
bids them pour out in oblation to purest
patriotism the life-blood of their hearts;
the shroud which spreads over the dead
of the battlefield Is the mantle of fame
and of glory-
Judge Emory Speer of Georgia fol
lowed with the closing address. At the j
conclusion of Judge Speer's address, I
which concluded the programme, there I
were loud calls of "McKinley, McKln- j
| ley!" The President had risen and j
was just about to leave the box, but he <
turned and came back to the front. He i
waited for a moment until ex-Governor j
Richard Ogelsby brought the assem- j
blage to something resembling quiet, j
Then the President spoke as follows:
My fellow-citizens: I have been deeply '
moved by this demonsrtation. I have j
been deeply touched by the words of pat- I
rlotlsm that have been uttered by dis- ,'
tinguished gentlemen so eloquently in j
your presence. It is gratifying to all of j
us to know that this has never ceased ]
to be a war of humanity. The last ship >
that went out of the harbor of Havana
before war was declared was an Ameri
can ship that had taken to the suffering
people of Cuba supplies furnished by (
American charity. (Applause.) And the '
first ship to- sail into the harbor of San
tiago was another American ship bearing
food supplies to the suffering Cubans.
(Applause.) And I am sure it is the uni
versal prayer of American citizens that
justice and humanity and civilization
BbaU characterize the final settlement of
peace, as they have distinguished the
progress of the war. (Applause.)
My countrymen, the currents of des
tiny flow through the hearts of the peo
ple. Who will check them, who will
divert them, who will stop them? And
the movements of men, planned and j
designed by the master of men, will j
never be interrupted by the American
people.
During the afternoon five meetings
were held in different parts of the city.
A large meeting at Studebaker Hall
was addressed by Albert J. Beveridge
of Indianapolis and President Cyrus
Northrup of the University of Chicago.
At the Columbia Theater General Miles,
General Henry M. Duffield of Detroit
and Booker T. Washington addressed
an audience limited only by the size
of the theater.
At the First Regiment Armory Secre
tary James Wilson and Mayor David S.
Rose of Milwaukee comprised the list
of speakers. Samuel Gompers spoke at
the Second Regiment Armory and
Charles Emory Smith delivered an ad
dress at North Side Turner Hall. The
public schools, all of which were pro
fusely decorated, devoted the day to
jubilee exercises and to hearing ad
dresses by prominent educators and or
ators.
GRAND BALL TO AID
SOLDIERS AND SAILORS
CHICAGO, Oct. 18.â Four thousand
-eople danced at the Auditorium to
night for the benefit of the suffering
sailors and soldiers, and it was a per
fect success. A small festoon of Ameri
can beauty roses hu.i? before the box
from which President McKinley viewed
the ball, and directly opposite a similar
decoration was Placed beneath the box
in which the Chinese and Korean Min
isters were seated. The hour for the
reception of the President and the dis
tinguished guests was 10:30 p. m. The
hall was draped with red, white and
blue strips across the entire roof.
Punctual to the minute the President
appeared. Chairman Truax and the
jubilee committee were also present.
Mayor Harrison wai on his left. The
orchestra rendered "The Star Spangled
Banner," and all in the hall remained
standing as the Presidential party
made its way across the floor.
The ceremony of presenting being
over the President made his way to the
box reserved for him, the audience ap
plauding enthusiastically. He left the
hall after midnight, going to the resi
dence of Captain Williams. About $25 -
000 was realized for the benefit of the
soldiers and sailors.
Professor Cox of the Signal Service
Bureau to-day had little in the way of
cheering prognostications for the next
thirty-six hours to offer Chicagoaris,
who have been hoping for fair weather
to-morrow, the day of the big parade
He said there would be "unsettled
weather, with rain, Wednesday."
PRESIDENT M'KINLEY
MEETS THE MIDDIES
CHICAGO, Oct. 18.â About 11:30 the
President left the Auditorium and was
driven to the hall of the Illinois Naval
Reserves, in the Medinah Temple,
where several naval reserves all in
uniform had gathered. As the Presi
dent, arm and arm with Mayor Harri
son, and accompanied by Captain Mc-
Calla of the Marblehead, Admiral
Brown, Commodore Ide, Captain W. J.
Wilson, Lieutenant J. A. Ãbdell and
Ensigns W. D. Flood and D. R. Col
lins, came on to the floor of the hall
they were greeted with hearty and pro
longed cheering. For ten minutes the
middies filed past the PresWent, shak
ing his hand. Then taking a position
at the end of the hall he addressed the
blue Jackets briefly. While the cheer
ing still continued the President ex
cused himself and departed. He was
driven at once to the McWllliams resi
dence on Lake avenue and retired soon
after.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 19, 1898.
MAJOR TILDEN
HAS RETURNED
Tells of Affairs at
Manila.
INSURGENTS NOT DANGEROUS
THEY WOULD HAVE NO CHANCE
AGAINST AMERICANS.
The California Officer Praises the
Bravery of the Call's Special
Correspondent During the
Fighting.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VICTORIA, B. C. Ov-t. 18.â On board
the steamer Empress of Japan, which
arrived this afternoon from the Orient,
was Major Tilden of the First Califor
nia Volunteers and Dr. Lewis of the
Red Cross Society, two well-known
San Franciscans who are on their way
home from Manila on leave.
Major Tilden says things are still In
statu quo at Manila, in fact they have
changed but little since the armistice
was declared. As for the fears of
trouble between the Americans and the
Philippine insurgents, he thinks that
there is scarcely any need for uneasi
ness on that score, for although the in
surgents might have done well with
the backing of the Americans against
the Spanish, they would not last longer
than an iceberg at the equator In a
conflict with the Americans.
A large number of Callfornlans, some
sick and some wounded, are en route
home, the major says, on the hospital
ship Rio de Janeiro, which left Hong
kong several days before the Empress.
He speaks in glowing terms of Sol
N. Sheridan, The Call's correspondent,
whom he met for the first time under
fire in the rebel trenches, where, not
withstanding the danger, the brave cor
respondent had gone in search of re
liable news for his paper.
The Empress of Japan brings a bud
get of Manila news. There was con
siderable excitement there prior to the
departure of the last mail for Hong
kong over the reported sale of Palawan
and part of the Sulu group to Germany
by Spain. The story of the sale was,
however, afterward denied by the Ger
man Consul, but still many of the Ori
ental papers refused to take his denial,
and many lengthy editorials appeared

RUSSIA WILL
UPHOLD FRANCE
Czar Nicholas' Congratnla
tions on the Firmness
Shown at Fashoda.
PARIS, Oct. 18.â The Soir as
serts that the forthcoming visit
of the Russian Foreign Minister,
Count Muravieff, to Paris will be
undertaken on the instructions
of Czar Nicholas in order to con
gratulate the French Foreign
Minister upon the firmness he
has shown in the Fashoda affair
and to arrange as to the manner
in which Russia can give her
support most effectively.
at Hongkong and Shanghai baaed upon
the news.
News is given by the correspondent of
the Hongkong Press of the sale of large
quantities of firearms to the rebels by
American merchants in Hongkong and
other Chinese ports. Several filibuster
ing expeditions are said to have suc
cessfully landed large quantities of

AMERICAN AND GERMAN
SAILORS IN STREET FIGHT
VICTORIA, B. C, Oct. 18.â Officers of
the steamer Empress of Japan report
that a serious street fight occurred at
Hongkong shortly before they left be
tween American and German sailors.
W. C. Lake, an American storekeeper,
has been murdered at Chemulpo.
From Hongkong comes the news of a
robbery of 10,000 yen from H. M. S. Nar
cissus by blue jacket'- who escaped on
a Japanese steamer bound for Yoko
hama. The steamer was pursued and
overtaken by a torpedo boat destroyer
and the sailors captured.
The steamer Borneo, which arrived at
Singapore about September 15, reported
passing while in the open sea off the
west coast of the Hebrides, through
showers of volcanic ashes for two days.

DIED FROM EATING
POISONED FOOD
Fate of Aged Mrs. Rus
sell at Fresno.
NARROW ESCAPE OF HER SON
PARTOOK OF BOTH MTTSH AND
CANNED OYSTERS.
Mush Made of Refuse Meal by the
Old Lady, Whose Son Had
Bought It for Chicken
Feed.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
FRESIw, Oct. 18.â An old lady, Mrs.
Resilla Russell, died at her home on
Harvey avenue, Belmont Addition, this ;
evening from the effects of eating
poisoned food. It is not known whether
her death was due to the eating of
tainted oysters or poisonous mush. It
is quite evident, however, that her
death was paused by partaking of ;
either the oysters or the mush, as she
became deathly sick shortly after
breakfast and an invalid son of the old
lady was similarly affected, but in his
case the illness was of a milder form.
Last night one of her sons brought
home from a restaurant some canned
oysters. The can had been opened and
a quantity of the contents used. The j
son thought, that the remainder would j
be nice for his aged mother. It is quite
likely that the oysters became tainted
on account of their exposure to the air.
It might have been the mush, however,
that was the death-dealing food, as one
of the sons had bought the meal, which
was refuse stuff, for chicken food. He
did not intend that it should have been
cooked, but he placed it where his
mother saw it and as she was near
sighted she made mush of it, which she
and the son who was taken sick ate.
She died several hours afterward.

SAN JOSE, Oct. 18.â Angus E. Cameron
died here to-day, aged S3. He was a na

known relatives, and the Coroner has
taken charge of his effects. He leaves
some property, just how much is not
known, though It Is stated that It
amounts to as much as $30,000 or $40,000.

GAGE GREETED
WITH GREAT
ENTHUSIASM
Sacramentans More Than Ea^er
to Listen to the Republican
Leader.
Discussed the Gubernatorial Nominee of the
Parties of Confusion and Exposed His
Treacherous Record.
SACRAMENTO, Oct. 18.â Henry T.
Gage, Republican nominee for Gover
nor, arrived in the capital city of Cali
fornia at 3 o'clock this afternoon. He
came from the mountain counties of
Calaveras and Amador, where the
meetings surpassed all previous politi
cal demonstrations in that region.
The enthusiasm for the Republican i
standard bearer was not confined to the !
meetings, but was exhibited by the j
people as Mr. Gage journeyed through j
the Sacramento Valley to-day.
Among the miners in the mountains, i
as among the farmers and fruit grow
ers of the San Joaquin Valley, he
gained outspoken supporters by reason
of his manly and unassuming manner i
In meeting and talking with the peo- j
pie. Mr. Gage is the kind of a man
that the masses respect. They recog
nize in him a man of the people, dif- j
ferent from the office-holding aristo
crats who have grown sleek and fat at
the public treasury.
The tour of the Sacramento Valley
opens under the most favorable aus
pices. In expectation of the coming
of the standard bearer the Republican
organization made ready to receive j
him, but no one anticipated that the
reception would take the form of a
great popular uprising, such as was ex- j
hibited in Sacramento to-night.
A committee of leading Republicans ;
went down the road to Gait and there j
met the standard bearer on his return j
from the mountains. Immediately on j
his arrival at Sacramento he was es- !
corted to the Golden Eagle Hotel. An
informal reception lasting two hours, !
from 3 o'clock until 5, was held at the \
hoteL Seven or eight hundred citi- i
zens of the capital city clasped the i
hand of the man of the people.
So many came that Mr. Gage barely
had time to exchange a word with each
comer. Before dark delegations began j
to arrive in Sacramento from Folsom, i
Gait, Davisville and other places. It j
became apparent to the committee that \
the pavilion on Sixth street would not !
hold or begin to hold the vast multl- j
tude that was beginning to assemble j
to cheer the Republican standard- j
bearer and listen to the eloquence of i
the matchless orator, General W. H. L. j
Barnes.
Among the Republican organizations j
that participated in the procession
which escorted Gage and Barnes to the j
pavilion were: State Capital McKinley j
Club, W. G. Govan, president; Army j
and Navy Republican League, Colonel j
George M. Mott, commander; Resolute
Club, Major W. H. Sherburn and Harry i
Rogers, president and vice president; I
Club of Ninety-Eight, F. F. Tebbets,
president; First Ward Club, W r . A. An- j
derson, president; Second Ward Club,
George Staacks; Third Ward Club,
William Hall; Fourth Ward Club, Wil
liam Avery; Fifth W r ard Club; Sixth
Ward Club, James Henderson; Seventh
Ward Club, W. D. Knight; Eighth
Ward Club, Thomas James; Ninth
Ward Club. J. H. Dolan; Oak Park
Club, L. S. Gertner; Gait Republican
Club, F. H. Harvey, president; Folsom
Republican Club, Captain Murphy;
Central Republican Club, Sacramento,
F. R. Dray.
The procession was handled by
George H. Clark, grand marshal.
The marching clubs in line. steDDine !
to the music of brass bands and drum
corps and saluted by the cheers of
thousands and thousands of spectators ;
who lined the street on either side of
the route, inspired the Republicans of
the capital. Rockets, red fire, banners I
and transparencies were features of I
the great demonstration. While count
less hundreds were viewing the parade J
those who knew a thing or two about i
the limited capacity of the old pavilion
went there early and secured seats.
When the head of the column reached
Sixth and M streets every chair in the
house was occupied and standing room
was at a premium. Later thousands
sought to gain admission, but they
were turned away. "An overflow meet
ing on Sixth street, near the pavilion,
was large and enthusiastic. It is esti
mated that 5000 people assembled on
the street near the pavilion entrance.
The vast assemblage in the pavilion
was called to order by A. J. Bruner.
chairman of the Republican County
Central Committee. He named as pres
ident of the occasion Captain W. H.
Stamens, commander of the Army and
Navy Republican League of California,
under the auspices of which the meet-
Ing was called. Mr. Bruner read the
followin- list of vice presidents:
Following are the names of the vice
presidents of the meeting:

Charles BaJne,
Captain W. H. Seamans, commander
of the Army and Navy Republican
League, spoke as follows:
Mr. chairman, comrades, ladies and
gentlemen: By the courtesy of the Coun
ty Central Committee, this meeting is
held under the auspices of the Army and
Navy Republican League. It is for this
reason, I suppose, that the chairman of
the committee has invited me, as the
head of the State League, to preside and
introduce the speakers of the evening.
The organization is a political one and
numbers more than 5000 active working
members in the State of California, and I
may say, fairly represents more than
nine-tenths of the 15,000 Union Veterans
In the State, who, with their family con
nections, will cast 20,000 votes at the com
ing election and place our friend Henry
T. Gage in the gubernatorial chair.
I am not expected to make a speech on
this occasion, and I shall not attempt It,
but I desire to say a word to my com
rades, many of whom 1 see in the audl-
Comrades, did it ever occur to you,
while fighting the battles of your coun
try upon land and upon sea, while in the
Bilent watches of the night froiy your
midnight bivouac you were thinking 1 of
home and friends, while suffering in nos
pitals or starving in Libby and Ander
sonville, that you would ever find an
American citizen so lost to all patriotic
sentiment and grateful remembrance aa
to assail your citizenship, apply to the
courts to disfranchise you and place you
upon the plane of paupers because by
virtuÂ© of wounds and disease incurred in
the service of your country, or from the <
infirmities of age, you might feel con- j
strained to accept the home provided for |
you by a beneficent government, where i
you might spend the remnant of your I
days in peace and comfort.
Huwever incredible it may appear to
you such a man has been found within
the limits of our own State, in a Demo- j
cratic leader who lives in Los Angeles !
County, where he has brought an action j
in the courts, which is now pending, to
disfranchise the Inmates of the National
Home at Santa Monica. Why? Because a
majority of our comrades there are Re
publicans and vote the Republican ticket.
Tiiis Democratic statesman aspires to
represent you in the upper house of Con
gress as the successor of the Hon. Stephen I
M. White should, by any possibility, I
the Democratic ticket be successful- at |
the coming election. Do you want him to
succeed?
My fellow-citizens, who are these men
that this Democratic leader would thus
rob of their citizenship; a right dear to
the heart of every old soldier? "They are
those who fought with Grant in the West;
who rode with Sheridan in the valley;
who marched with Sherman to the sea;
who were with Farragut, the invincible,
when he took his fleet of wooden ships
through that cyclone of shot and shell at
New Orleans, past the frowning batteries
of Vicksburg and Fort Hudson and into
the harbor of Mobile, teaching a lesson
in naval warfare which has enabled the
gallant Dewey, his lieutenant, to anni
hilate the Spanish fleet before Manila;
and Sampson, Schley, Clark of tho
Oregon, and fighting Bob Evans to sweep,
as with a West India hurricane. Cervera
and his ships from the face of the Carib
bean Bea.
They are thase who were at Gettys
burg, Petersburg, Chickamauga, Chan
cellorsville, Antietam, Shiloh and
Cold Harbor on that memorable
day in June, 1864, when 10,000 went
down in twenty minutes, and many
of them were participants in a hundred
other battles. These were no skirmishes,
but battles royal, in twelve of which 188,
000 men were lost to the Union arms.
This was war, grim-visaged- war, in which
350,000 of our comrades laid down their
lives and twice as many more were
maimed for life to the end that the na
tion might live.
Now, comrades, in these piping times
of peace, for the Spanish war is practi
cally over, you have duties to perform as
citizens only less important than those
rendered by you as soldiers of the repu
llc. and if you do your duty to your com
rades and to yourselves now as well as
you did it then you will relegate, this
Democratic statesman to private life and
save the State of California to the Re
publican party by your votes, on thft Bth
day of November, 1898, as you saved the
nation by your bayonets at Appomattox
on the 9th day of April, 1865. Will you do
it?
In closing, Captain Seamens intro
duced Henry T. Gage. The audience
responded with tremendous shouts of
applause and prolonged demonstrations
of welcome. When the tumult subsided
the standard bearer thanked the people
of Sacramento for the magnificent re
ception. He said he did not take the
greeting as personal, but regarded it as
an exhibition of the devotion of the
people to Republican principles.
There was an unmistakable design on
the part of persons in the pavilion, near
the entrance, to embarrass Mr. Gage
with noise and talking. Chairman Sea
mens, observing the interruptions, said
a few words to the audience, asking
that the privilege of talking be granted
exclusively to the regularly appointed
speakers.
Mr. Gage, after dwelling upon the
prospects of a great victory at the polls
on the Bth of November, proceeded to
v discussion of single tax problems. The
points against Maguire's pet scheme
were made in a style that challenged
the attention of his hearers and called
forth rounds of applause.
In a forcible manner Mr. Gage re
pelled Maguire's assault on the In
tegrity of the Republican State conven
tion.
The railroad settlement question was
considered and the Congressional Rec
ord produced to sustain the assertion
that Maguire did not object to the set
tlement at the time when objection
would have defeated the measure. Mr.
Gage did not ask the people to take his
word for this declaration, but invited
particular attention to the debate in
Congress. The points in this regard
were presented here in Sacramento as
they were in Los Angeles, San Diego,
Fresno, Stockton and other cities. Mr.
Gage dwelt -with special emphasis on
the fact that Senators Morgan and
White, recognized Democratic leaders,
scanned the measure carefully and sug
gested an amendment to give it greater
strength, and that the amendment so
perfected received the unanimous sup
port of every Republican, Democrat and
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 19, 1898.
Populist in the United States Senate.
Here, as elsewhere, the people listened
with profound attention to the story of
Maguire's double-dealing on the rail
road question.
In closing his masterly address the
standard-bearer talked in the plainest
fashion to the audience. He assured
the people that he would give them a
fearless, economic and clean adminis
tration. The assemblage applauded the
sentiments expressed in the promise.
The Sacramento Athletic Club Quar
tet entertained the audience and re
sponded to an encore. .
General W. H. L. Barnes was greeted
in a most cordial and enthusiastic man
ner by the vast audience.
After paying his respects to Judge
Maguire in a fashion that evoked peals
of laughter at the expense of the office
holding giant, allusion was made to
Maguire's refusal to discuss the money
question or the platforms which he
straddled; it was said that he simply
shouted railroad and sat on the fence
howling at the cars and making faces
at the conductor.
Maguire crankism engaged the atten
tion of the eloquent exponent of true
Republicanism, and the danger was in
dicated of electin- to high office of
Governor one who entertained sym
pathy and admiration for anarchists.
The beautiful word painting, the elo
quent tributes to American institutions,
and the noble flights of oratory which
distinguished the efforts of the speaker
in his recent Southern California tour
were features of his splendid speech at
the pavilion to-night. He inspired the
audience to a high degree of enthusi
asm. He closed with a superb perora
tion on the love of country, and won
mighty rounds of applause.
The meeting inside adjourned with
great cheers for Gage and xsefr ana w.
H. L. Barnes. ,
The outdoor meeting was addressed
by local talent. In every respect the
demonstration to-night far surpassed
the fusion gathering at the pavilion last
Saturday night. Not more than three
thousand people assembled to hear Ma
guire and Hutchlnson. It is estimated
than ten thousand people sought to
hear Gage and Barnes to-night, lhat
number assembled at Sixth and M
streets, hoping to gain admission to the
pavilion. The success of the demon
stration has inspired Republicans with
renewed confidence and zeal.
Gage and Barnes go to Auburn to

BIG DEMONSTRATION BY
POMONA REPUBLICANS
Largest Assemblage in the History
of the City Listens to Elo
quent Speakers.
POMONA. Oct. IS.-No political meeting
in Pomona Valley has ever compared with
that held here this evening by the Gage
and Neff Republican Club of Pomona. It
was the biggest and most enthusiastic
assemblage ever grouped in this locality.
Henry T. Gage has been known intimately
by hundreds of Pomona citizens for near
ly a quarter of a century, and no candi
date for political office was ever so uni
versally and thoroughly popular in Po
mona Valley as he. It has therefore been
an easy task to get up a mammoth meet-
Ing here for him and the whole Republi-
When the special train from Pasadena
bearing the famous Republican marchinff
club, the Americus, rolled Into Pomona
station, there was a roar ot saluting
cannon and cheers by the multitude.
From sunset till after 7 o'clock people
came into Pomona city by every rural
road and every sort of vehicle. The bu.si-
I ness streets were packed when the parade
! of the Americus Club and local Republi-
I cans clubs took place. As the white unt
! formed company proceeded down the
I streets a storm of hand clapping by the
jostling throngs of spectators accompan
ied the marchers.
Because the Pomona Opera House was too
small to hoid oven a fraction of the multl
! tude of people who had come to attend
i the meeting a large circus tent had been
hired, and when Chairman Albert Dolo
rose to call the meeting to order the tent
was jammed and packed with men and
women. So large an audience had never
before been seen here. Hundreds of men j
could not get ntarer than twenty foet of
the large tent and went home.
Russell J. Waters, Republican candidate
for Congress in the Sixth District, spoke
i half an hour on the issues in his canvass.
i Several times he was so tumultuously
cheered that he had to cease speaking for
a full minute or more. .
Will H. Harris, who has been
a lifelong Democrat and an old
Democratic stump speaker in Southern
California and who now supports the
whole Republican ticket, had such a
demonstration and enthusiastic reception
that he could hardly proceed with his
speech When in an eloquent passage he
referred to the President the multitude
rose and cheered and yelled for several
minutes.

TO REOPEN A GREAT REGISTER
Derelict Voters of San Joaquin to Be
Given a Chance.
STOCKTON, Oct. 18.â The great regis
ter of San Joaquin County must be re
opened. That was the decision rendered
this afternoon by Judge Jones in the pe
tition brought nominally by Milton Jack
son Johnson against the County Clerk for
a writ of mandate compelling him to en
ter the petitioner's name on the great
book of the county.
This decision opens up the way to the
registration of 128 names now filed await
ing the court's action. It is estimated
that 150 more names will be handed in.
The register will be reopened at 9 o'clock
to-morrow morning and continue open
until 5 o'clock p. m. next Monday.
SOLANO TEACHERS AGAINST IT
Oppose the Seventh Amendment to
the State Constitution.
SUISUN, Oct. 18.â The Solano County
Teachers' Institute has passed a resolu
tion opposing the seventh amendment to
the constitution of the State of California,
proposed for adoption this year. The
teachers believe that the amendment was
proposed in the interests of education, but
hold that an opposite effect wili result in
Its adoption and that the entire State
school system will suffer a serious in
jury.

BURNED TO DEATH.
Shocking Fate of an Aged Woman
at Petaluma.
PETALITMA, Oct. IS.â Last night while
Mrs. L. Brock, an aged woman residing
in thin city, waa working about her yard
her clothes took fire and before she could
do anything the flames enveloped her
completely. She rushed into the house,
Betting fire to the building. The prompt
arrival of neighbors resulted in the ex
tlnfruishlng of the flames, but it was too
late to save the sufferer, who died dur
ing the night.

Assemblyman Wright not only introduced the
bill but labored effectively for its passage. He
was indefatigable In getting it approved by
committees and In > smoothing down the objec
tions of members who were disposed to dispute
its passage. He worked as faithfully for the
University bill as an Assemblyman could work
for anything, and It Is a great mistake for My.
Bennett to try. to belittle the candidate against
him, especially in connection with this matter.
Aside from the University bill, Mr. ' Wright
made a record as a faithful member, and one
who was progressive, alert and Independent.
The voters of the Fifty-first District will do the
proper thing If they return Mr. Wright to the
Assembly.

BAIN IS BADLY NEEDED.
Southern California Climate and Crop
Bulletin.
LOS ANGELES, Oct. 18.â The United
States Department of Agriculture, Cli
mate and Crop Bulletin of the Weather
Bureau for Southern California, for the
week ending October 17, IS9B, reports:
The weather was generally warm and
dry during the past week, except In some
of the coast sections, where morning fogs
occurred. The warm weather was bene
ficial to raisin making, but It was detri
mental to orchards, which are suffering in
some places, though in others oranges and
lemons are doing well under the unfavor
able weather conditions, and well-tilled
loam soil will yield a crop of fine fruit.
Farm work continues at a standstill and
will remain so until the much-needed
rains come generously and copiously.

BUDD SPEAKS
FOR HARMONY
Asks Democrats to
Forget Grievances.
FUTURE RELIEF PROMISED
MASS MEETING LAST NIGHT AT
METROPOLITAN HALL.
Walter M. Stradley Gave It as His
Belief That M. H. de Young
Is Seeking Senatorial
Honors.
A large nnd enthusiastic meeting was
held at Metropolitan Hall lust night un
d'-r the auspices of the Maguire Lreague of
Clubs. Governor James H. Budd was
cimlrman of the evening- and Walter P.
Stradley the orator of the occasion.
Stradley spoke for nearly two hours on
the State and national issues of the cam
paign as viewed from a Democratic
standpoint. He was frequently inter
rupted by outbursts of applause. Gov
ernor Budd made one of his usual happy
addresses, and received an ovation when
be appeared on the stage.
The meeting was called to order by
Oscar Hooks.
Alfred Donton Crldge of Hanford made
a brief addnss in which he predicted the
success of Maguire and James H. Barry
at the polls.
John P. Dunn, candidate for member of
the State Bi ard "f Equalization, was
next introduced, but had not proceeded !
far in his remarks when he was cut short I
by the arrival of Governor liudd, Walter
P. Stradley, William P. Lawlor, T. B.
Treacy and Charles H. Holcomb. presi
dent of the Maguire League of Clubs, j
Preceding- them was a band playing mar
tial airs, while behind them came sev
eral hundred members of the League un
der the marshalship of James D. Powers.
There was not sufficient room to accom
modate the new arrivals with seats, and
>f them were compelled to stand on
the platform and in the side a.
:: Governor Budd was Introduced
.irman, he was greeted with a storm {
*-rs. He said:
Well, It does not appear to me as though i
any soiit in the Democratic party has lost ue
any Democrats. [Applause.] This 1s the larg
est meeting that I In my exrorience have ever
seen gathered in or in the vlcintly of this hall.
[Applause.]
Later in the campaign I intend to address
the citizens of this city on the issues, at least
what I consider issues of the present campaign.
At the present time it is my duty to preside
over this meeting and introduce to you the
gentlemen selected to discuss those Issues to
night. There is one thins-, however, which con
cerns the Democratic party of this city more
than any other. We may differ upon methods
In the past; we may differ upon procedure that
haÂ« been had, but we are now. lined up bÂ»
fore a common enemy and it Is the duty of
every man who claims that he* belongs to the
democratic party and holds its success dear to
his nrt to either get in behind the Demo
cratic banner or walk rut of the party forever.
Goverr.cr Budd thon addressed himself
to the Senatorial fight. He called special i
attention to the Importance of electing a
Democratic United States Senator.
Walter P. Stradley, who followed Gov
ernor Budd. dealt principally with the f
financial issues of the national campaign. |
He said that the Democratic party was |
In the right to stay until the> single gold I
standard was driven into the sea. He j
then said that the opposition was black- j
guarding- and vilifying James G. Ma- j
quire because they feared him. "If elect- !
el Governor," said he. "Judge Maguire i
will not be under corporation control or I
rule. He will give a government for the
people and by the people."
In dealing with the recent war with
Spain Stradley said that in his opinion it
was unavoidable and that it had been
fought by the American people on non
partisan lines.
Edward J. Woodman, candidate for
State Printer, and Edward L. Fitzgerald, j
nominee for Public Administrator, made !
ehort speeches, after which the meeting
adjourned with cheers for Budd, Stradley
and Maguire. "â¢â â .:â¢-â¢'â 
MARTIN REPUBLICAN CLUB.
Flans of Organization Being Rapidly
Carried Out by the Members.
The Henry S. Martin Republican Club
ha-s nearly completed its plans of organ
ization, and when they are fully car
ried out the Republican candidate for
Sheriff will find himself backed by one of
the strongest individual political organi
zations in the city. At a meeting held
last evening In Native Sons' Hall there
were placed on record the names of those
selected by committees from the various
districts to act as chairmen of the differ
ent clubs organized to labor in Henry S.
Martin's interest. By Saturday night it
is expected that complete organization
will have been affected, and then work for
Martin's election will commence in ear
nest.
A W. A. Beane Rally.
A rousing meeting of the "William A.
Deane Club was held at Becker Hall last
night. President Fuhrman in the chair.
Reports from the various district com
mittees were gratifying, showing every
where gains for the popular candidate.
The meeting was addressed by D. Shaben,
P. W. Lannon, J. Coffrey, C. F. Kapp,
John Heenan. Samuel Rothberg, Dr.
Bohm and others.
