THE MAYOR AS HE IS
AND AS HE APPEARS
WOULD REDUCE
THE WAGES OF
THE LABORER
Says Lamp-Lighters Should Be
Paid Twenty-Five Dol
lars a Month.
QUESTION ASKED BY DISCREDITED
DEMOCRATS.
Plain Facts Concerning the Way in
Which the Tearing Up of Street
Car Tracks on Market Street
Was Done.
Since James D. Phelan, the "heft
ing" Mayor, constituted himself the
Democratic party of San Francisco, and
by his acts and declarations has given
publicity to his belief that he is the
only honest man within the confines of
the State, there has been a growing
desire in the minds of the voters of
the city to know more of the man who
appeared as a political bud two years
ago and has now blossomed into a full
blown boss who does not want anything
but office.
Unfortunately for Mr. Phelan. this
curiosity on the part of the public has
resulted in bringing to light facts that
are not at all to his credit. The pic
ture of Mr. Phelan, as presented by the
facts, when contrasted with the like
ness of the "hefting" Mayor, as etched
by his own ready tongue, shows him
to be a mean and petty schemer; a man
who, though wealthy, resorts to the
most miserable subterfuges to further
add to his wealth at the expense of the
public; a man who has used his offi
cial position to promote his personal in
terests, and a man who is the willing
tool of designing persons who do not
hesitate to delude the public in every
way possible, if by doing so they can
further their own selfish Interests.
So marked is the difference between
Phelan, the candidate for office, and
Phelan, the office holder, that the con
trast is apparent even without the most
cursory examination of the man. The
one dons a slouch hat and an air of
good fellowship and goes among the
laboring men with a tale of his love
for them and his earnest wish to pro
mote their welfare; the other sits on
his throne In the City Hall and makes
public declaration that he believes $2o
a month is adequate remuneration for
the services of the men who light the
city's gas lamps.
Be it understood that the grasping
corporation that employs the gas lamp
lighters â the men who cover the end
less miles of city streets through wind
and rain and fog every night and
morning in the year, that the safety
of the after-night pedestrian may be
assured â pays them but a pittance of
$40 a month for their hire. This fact
came to light during the proceedings
that attended the recent fixing of gas
and water rates by the Board of Ku
pervisors. The admission was made
by an official of the gas company.
Those who heard it, having in mind
the Mayor's ante-election utterances*,
expected* him to launch forth in a with
ering condemnation of the greed that
induced the corporation to make such
miserable recompense for faithrul ser
vice. Nothing of the kind. The Mayor
gave evidence of great Indignation, it
is true, but it was for a different rea
son. He announced with some show
of spirit that to pay the lamp lighters
$40 a month was nothing more or less
than gross extravagance, and that in
his opinion the sum of $25 a month was
amply sufficient.
Think of it! A beggarly $25 a month
for a laboring man, and out of this
he must pay house rent, provide for his
family and rear and educate his chil
dren that they may become good
American citizens. And this from a
man who is the possessor of millions,
through no effort of his own, and the
Mayor of a great city that boasts r.f
being the choicest blossom in \he gar
den spot of the world. A Chines*
cook who would accept such a sum for
his monthly wage would be boycotted
by his tong â and rightly so.
It Is in connection with this same
proceeding of fixing the gas rates that
THE SA^T FEANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 19, 1898.
another query is being made, and It is
one that perhaps the Mayor will see his
way clear to answer and thus set at
rest a grave suspicion that may result
In the loss to him of many of the votes
he is so industriously seeking. It will
be remembered that the Mayor vetoed
the gas rate as fixed by the Board of
Supervisors. Possibly he was justified
In so doing, as it is not recorded that a
corporation like the gas company has
ever done less than rob the city in the
matter of rates. But this fact does not
answer the query, which is, in brief:
"Was not the Mayor's act in vetoing
the gas rate influenced by the fact that
he is a large holder of stock In a rival
gas company that would be greatly
benefited if paid a subsidy and given
the contract of lighting the city's
streets and public buildings?" The
ways of Providence and "hefting"
Mayors are inscrutable.
Many persons have watched, with
varying degrees of interest, Mr. Phe
lan's efforts to make capital out of his
action in ordering the removal of the
horse-car tracks on Market street and,
incidentally, the subsequent paving of
that thoroughfare. To those who are
acquainted with the facts the compla
cency with which the Mayor has ab
sorbed the compliments that a subsi
dized press has showered upon him in
connection with this matter, as well as
the manner in which he has arrogated
to himself all the credit that attached
to the action, has been instructive. It
shows the man in a different light from
that in which, without doubt, he views
himself and his actions, and in which
he wishes the public to see him.
The facts are these: Some time be
fore the removal of the tracks was ac
complished Governor Budd sat at lunch
with a party of friends at a down-town
club. One of these friends was a well
known attorney who, by the way, is
also a very prominent Republican.
During the course of the round-the
table talk the subject of street railways
was introduced. The attorney, who is
a recognized authority on matters per
taining to street railways, stated that
he had recently made an exhaustive
study of the point and had ascertained
that there was no law to warrant the
maintenance of four car tracks on Mar
ket street by the Market Street Rail
way Company. A few days later Gov
ernor Budd called on the managing
editor of the Examiner and detailed the
conversation just referred to. "Here^is
a chance for you to make some political
capital, " said the Governor. "Phelan
Is your man and you can advise him to
tear up those tracks and he will get
the credit for it. Send down to Judge
Brldgford and get an opinion from him.
If the opinion corresponds with what
I have told you turn Phelan loose and
he will get the credit."
E. A. Bridgford represented Colusa
County in the last Assembly, but is
now a member of the Committee of
One Hundred. He was called into con
sultation and after studying the law on
the subject rendered an opinion in ac
cordance with what Governor Budd had
been told by his friend at the club.
Phelan was then taken into the con
fidence of his protectors and told to go
ahead and win glory and the undying
gratitude of an appreciative public.
Such is a true statement of how the
Mayor lays awake nights studying out
plans for the betterment of the masses.
It may also be stated in passing that
the tearing up of the tracks and the
subsequent paving of the street added
materially to the value of Phelan's
property on Market street.
Like all true tales there Is a sequel
attached to Mr. Phelan's act in clearing
Market street of the horse-car tracks.
The tracks having been disposed of the
question of repavlng the street waa
next in order. Bitumen was the kind of
pavf-ment selected for doing the work.
It is here that query No. 2 comes in.
It has been propounded by many curi
ous people who have, during the past
three weeks, watched the laying of the
block pavement in front of Mr. Phe
lan's premises on Market street. These
curious people want to know what in
ducement was offered by the proprie
tors of the block pavement company to
enable them to secure the use of sev
eral hundred feet of the principal
thoroughfare of the city for the pur
pose of setting up a sample of the goods
they have for sale. It would be as in
teresting to know if the Mayor owns
stock in the block pavement company
as it would be to know if his official
acts have been unconsciously influenced
by the fact that he holds stock in an
opposition gas company.
The spirit of Inquiry is In the air.
The Democratic voters of San Fran
cisco have been debarred from having
any voice in the selection of their can
didates for office on the ground that
they are unworthy of such a trust. This
being so It is not without reason that
these same voters should make inquiry
as to the purity of the man who has
impugned their honesty and good inten
tions. Up to the present time the in
quiry has not developed anything to
the credit of the man who has consti
tuteu himself the Democratic party of
San Francisco.
PATTON THEIR CHOICE.
The Municipal League Adopts Reso
lutions Complimentary to the
Republican Candidate.
The Municipal League of San Francisco
met at its headquarters yesterday after
noon for the purpose of naming a ticket.
C. G. Nagle presided.
A motion was made that 100,000 tickets
be ordered printed and circulated con
taining the names of those nominees se
lected by the league. The roll call showed
107 delegates present.
The following ticket was selected:
Mayor, Charles L. Patton; Auditor, Asa
R. Wells; Diwtrict Attorney, Daniel J.
Murphy; Treasurer, I. J. Truman; Sheriff,
Henry S. Martin; Public Administrator,
John A. Drlnkhoupe; County Clerk, Wil
liam A. Deane; Superintendent of Streets.
Alartin F. Fragley; City and County At
torney, \V. F. Fitzgerald, Recorder, A. C.
Bi-rthier; Surveyor, Charles S. Tilton; Tax
Collector, Ed I. Sheehan.
The following resolution waa adopted:
Whereas, Charles L. Patton, the Republican
nominee for Mayor of the city and county .if
San Francisco, is a Native Son of the Golden
West and respected by all good citizens; and
whereas, Charles L. Patton Is well known In
our city for muny acts of charity and kind
ness in helping the poor and worthy of our
city; and whereaa, he Is a citizen who stands
for Improvement and the advancement of our
city, and la an advocate of the reforms neces
sary to accomplish that end: and whereas. It
Is ono of the principles of this league to advo
cate reform where needed and to advance the
election of worthy and well-known citizens,
therefore be It
Resolved, That we Indorse Charles L. Pat
ton as our nominee for the office of Mayor of
the city and County of San Francisco, and we
hereby pledge him our Individual and collec
tive support In the coming election, and It Is
ordered that he be placed at the head of our
ticket.
The indorsement of the remainder of the
ticket was laid over until to-morrow
evening.
Official Republican Club.
A large and enthusiastic audience filled
the new and elegant quarters of the Of
ficial Republican Club of the Thirty-fifth
Assembly District at Irving Hall, corner
Valencia and Hill streets, last evening.
Permanent officers of the organization
were elected as follows: President,
Thomas C. Maher; secretary, Joseph J.
Guilfoyle; assistant secretary. John A.
Shopston Jr.; treasurer, Andrew J.
Piercy; executive committee, I. H.
Thompson, John D. Daly, Robert H. Staf
ford, Joseph Wurdrow, E. A. Schumacher,
Edward J. Smith and John Meyers.
A Julius Kahn Club.
A Julius Kahn Club of the Twenty
ninth and Thirtieth Assembly Districts
was regularly formed at 836 Howard
street last evening. The following spea'k
frs addressed the club: Julius Kahn, W.
. Hawkins and Charles Kenneally.
AN EVENING
WITH MANY OF
THE CANDIDATES
A Rousing Republican
Meeting.
CHARLES L PATTON SPEAKS
CHEERS FOR HENRY MARTIN,
McKINXAY AND MTJRPHY.
Interesting Addresses Made by Those
Who Will Go Into Office
After the Coming
Election.
The people of the western part of the
city, those living near Golden Gate Park,
had an excellent opportunity of meeting
and getting acquainted with the Republi
can candidates lust evening. A rousing
meeting was held under the auspices of
the Associated League of United Labor
Clubs in Cyclery Hall, 506 Stanyan street,
at which Edward Howe presided.
Long before the time for calling the as
sembly to order every chair In the big
apartment was occupied by people whose
Republican enthusiasm could not be
doubted, Judging from the manner In
which every candidate was welcomed as
he entered the hall. There was plenty of
musical talent there as well. Probably
what attracted the most attention v. as
the anouncement that Charles L. Pat
ton, candidate for Mayor, and D. A.
Mc&lnlay of Santa Rosa were to be pres
ent and address the gathering.
Theso were by no means the only per
sons who aspire to office. In tact th^
whole platform was tilled with candi
dates and none left feeling that lie 1 had
not been welcomed. The first to speak
was Mr. McKlnlay, who said that he pre
ferred to discuss matters of national and
State importance than those municipal.
He said that this campaign is being con
ducted upon entirely new lines from any
in the past. All sectionalism had been
â wiped out forever by the recent war
where North and South fought side by
side for the honor of the nation and the
glory of the flag. He urged his audience
to uphold the administration by sending
baok to Congress men who would see that
none but Republicans are favored at the
hands of the legislator*. As a reason why
the present administration should be up
held he cited the fact that since President
McKlnley went Into office more men :iro
at work and more factories are in oper
ation than for many a long month pre-
The' speaker paid his respects to Candi
date Maguire, saying that the latter has
endeavored to make the people believe
that the railroad was the only issue in
the present campaign. That ie not so.
If such was the case. Mr. Maguire would
be asking to be sent back to Congress, In
order to get his name into the papers as
an anti-railroad man. Instead of urelnK
the people to elect him to the position
of Governor of this State. Mr. McKln
lay also pulled the candidate for Gov
ernor over the coals on the single tax
proposition.
\\ hile he was speaking there was a
great shout in the roar ot the hall, which
announced the arrival of Mr. Patton. Tho
latter took the platform at once, and In
one of his simple characteristic speeches
told the audience what they might ex
pect of him if they elect him to tho high
position of Mayor of this city. Ills posi
tion in favor of the new charttr, dol
lar limit, public ownership of public utili
ties, park Improvements, etc., were stated
clearly and met with Urn approval of
those whom he addressed. At the con
clusion of his short address Mr. Patton
said that the Republican party will d>
more for the laboring man than the Dem
ocratic party will, and finished by refut
ing the statement that he is the creature
of any boss or corporation.
D. J. Murphy, candidate for District At
torney, made a rousing speech, in which
he dlaeusst'd the merits of the many who,
under the Republican banner, are seek
ing the votes of the people. Before fin
ishing he had a few words to say of hi
political opponent, Barclay Henley, who
he declared is not? to be trusted, judging
from the man's past life. Henry S. Mar
tin, who aspires to be the next Sheriff,
received an ovation that would flatter
any one. The crowd cheered him from
the time he entered the hall until he left.
Among the other candidates present
who made a few remarks were Edward
H. Algeltinger for Supervisor, John D.
Siebe tor Assessor, Martin F. Fragley for
Superintendent of Streets, Charles Curry
for Secretary of State, William A. Deane
for County Clerk, 1. J. Truman for Treas
urer, A. C. Berthler for Recorder Ed
ward I. Sheehan for Tax Collector.
Thomas Graham for Police Judge, Police
Judge C. A. Low for re-election, Thomas
F. Dunn for Justice of the Peace and sev
eral others. â¢ It was voted as one of the
Golden Gate Republicans.
The Golden Gate Republican Club met
laÃt night at the lower hall of the Met
ropolitan Temple. M. H. Morgan pre
sided. The club was addressed by W. A.
Kemp, who Bald that although a nominee
for School Director on the Republican
ticket he was not going: to make a tirade
against the present board.
Frank Brill then took the platform and
placed Julius Kahn in nomination for the
club's indorsement as Congressman, which
was given with a will, amid cheers. Mr.
Brill claimed that It was the most im
portant part of the election to strengthen
the President's hand by electing a Repub
lican Congressman.
Lawrence Mulranen placed the name of
J. G. Edwards before the club for its in
dorsement to the position of member of
the State Board of Equalization. He
dwelt at some length upon the merits of
the candidate, and his ability to (111 the
position. The indorsement was made by
acclamation.
The club intends to hold a smoker next
Tuesday evening to which all of the Re
publican clubs will be invited.
Another Labor Ticket.
Registrar Higgy Is In a quandary over
the receipt of another United Labor tic
ket, acompanied by 3311 names. Political
Manager Grace 1b the sponser for this
one, and he informed the Registrar that
the other United Labor ticket presented
by Attorney Nagle was not the real ar
ticle. The Nagle ticket has a Democratic
aspect, while that of Grace is a sort of
omelet of all parties. It is claimed that
certain candidates have been offered
places on the ticket for $75 a head. One
of them made this statement to the Reg
istrar, but the latter declines to glvo
names. It is openly alleged that the
multiplicity of tickets in this campaign
had been caused by an overweening de
sire on the part of certain politicians to
Bell places thereon to the highest bidders.
Meeting of Republicans.
The regular weekly meeting of the
Fortieth Assembly District Republican
Club will be held at the corner of Post
and Fillmore streets this evening at S
o'clock. The voters of the district, with
their lady and gentlemen friends, are ex
pected to turn out in force, as Charles
L Patton and other prominent candidates
for local offices will be present to ad
dress the assemblage. Owing to the
large number of aspirants for political
honors on the municipal ticket and their
desire to show themselves and meet the
voters of this district, these weekly meet
ings will continue up to November 4. on
which date the club will hold a grand
rally and mass meeting at the Franklin
Hall. .

The committee promised to immediately
begin work on the municipal nominees
and continue the same until completed.

Independents Will Rally.
The Independent Club of the Fourth
District will hold a grand rally this even

ing.
POET RILEY PAINTED
SIGNS ON FENCES
WAS IN PARTNERSHIP WITH
WALLACE THE SHOWMAN.
A Reminiscence of Their Early Days
in Peru, Indiana, When Both
Were Poor.
Some thirty odd years ago Showman B.
E. Wallace was in partnership for a brief
season with James Whltcomb Rlley. At
that time James was not a developed
poet and Wallace was not the proprietor
of a big circus. The latter tells the story
in this way:
"Rlley came tramping into Peru, Ind.,
one day. He was out at the elbows, out
at the knees and generally out of luck.
He came to me for assistance. I was run
ning a small livery stable, and it was a
1 hard Job to make ends meet. So when
Riley, who was an utter stranger, braced
me for a horse and buggy for several
days, saying he had a scneme to make us
both richer, 1 was in considerable doubt
as to whether 1 should venture or not.
Riley stated that he was a painter by
trade, and that he would contract with
tea or more of the merchants in town to
pafnt< signs on the farmers' fences alor> nr
j the country roads announcing that 'This
is the way to Jones's!' and 'Smith has
the best boots!' etc. He would charge $1
apiece for the signs and paint at least
forty of them, provided that 1 would find
him in white paint, pay for his lodging at
a hotel and give him a buggy for several
days, taking naif of the gross receipts for
compensation.
"Trie fellow was so earnest and looked
po hungry that, for the life of me, I could
not refuse him, so at the business we
i weiit. I secured the paint and guaranteed
i his bill at one of the small hotels. I also
i loaned him a horse and buggy. He went
j at the Job with a will and soon secured
! contracts from half of the merchants In
town to decorate the country fences. At
that time roadside signs were Just begin
> nlng to come into use, and around Peru
none had been painted up to the time of
Rlley's advent. But, bless you. it wasn't
long before the whole region was bedeck
ed with trite sentences telling- of the won
; derful cheapness of the goods of Smith
and Jones. The merchants were well
pleased, especially as on some of the
signs there was a display of rhythmic
sentences like this. To secure sound sleep
buy of Cheep.' Rlley took in $40 within
1 two days, for he was a swift artist with
the brush, and I received half and was
Wfll repaid for my venture. Rlley went
from town to town painting signs upon
the fence boards and earning a comfort
able living, I have no doubt.
"Only last year near Peru some carpen
; ters were tearing down a barn that bad
been built up against an old fence, and
they came upon one of Rlley's signs, long
obscured from view and with the paint
I comparatively bright, tilling the multi
tude to go to Jones' cheapest store on
earth.
"Things have changed since th<">=e (lays.
Riley made his reputation as a poet and
did not have to paint fences any longer.
1 got out of thp llv.-rv business and began
; running a show, ami my former partner
was seen no more. But I sometimes think
1 of him and wonder what wo-ild have been
, his fate If he had not had that rhyming
twist to his brain. Perhaps he would
! have been painting fences all of his days."

CROTHERS WEEKLY EXPLAINS.
THERE has been some doubt among the few
who pay attention to the attitude of the Bul
letin on political questions as to the excuse
Crothers has for his conduct. Others among the few
have never had any doubts.
The Bulletin says it favors Julius Kahn for Con
gress because he is the nominee of the Republican
party. An excellent reason, Mr. Crothers, a con
vincing reason, one which does your perspicuity
proud. You might have added that he happened to
be the best man for the position.
But the Bulletin in its feeble way is for Phelan for
Mayor. Why? Phelan is not the nominee of the
Republican party. He is a Democrat. It might al
most be said that he is THE Democrat. He is the
whole party, brains and appetite. Surely he is not
being supported because he is a Republican, or do
the Bulletin heathen in their blindness bow down
to the little god of Democracy and not realize what
they are doing? If the fact that Kahn is a Republican
is a sufficient reason for supporting him, it ought to
be sufficient reason for supporting the whole ticket.
From the diadem of the Bulletin editor there is lack
ing the jewel of consistency.
When a paper throws its trifle of influence to one
man because he is a Republican, and at the same time
whoops things up for another candidate who is on the
other ticket, it ought to be more explicit. Other
wise unkind people will think that it has been pur
chased. Such a suspicion would be painful to a per
son so delicately sensitive as Crothers. Another ex
planation is due, even overdue. The Bulletin has
given no reason for being Phelan's creature. It must
have a reason. Why not be frank? If there was a
price paid there would be a $Ã¼bdued $atisfaction in
knowing what Crothers thinks himself worth.
SWEARS TO ITS OWN DECfIY.
THERE are few occasions on which any neces
sity appears for believing anything the Exam
iner may say, but we are inclined to think it
truthful when it swears to its loss of circulation. For
months it has been going down hill, and with
tender regret we have called attention to the fact.
During September the daily circulation, according
to its own affidavit maker, was 2184 less than during
August. This is a great decline. Details are lack
ing, however. There is no showing of what part of
this circulation went to the garbage crematory. Such
circulation may justly be termed "paid," for the
burning of it entails an expense of 20 cents a cubic
yard. â¢
While this falling off has taken place the Examiner
has been making desperate efforts, through the pub
lication of stupid puzzles and the promise of prizes,
to popularize itself. Doubtless this scheme has re
sulted in the sale of many extra copies, and without
it the showing would have been worse by far for the
paper itself, though better for the community.
When it is reflected that the present Board of Su
pervisors is made up of the personal selections of
the Mayor, when the fact is recalled that it has been
corrupt and inefficient, the circumstance that the
Mayor declares there are four wards in the city from
which no fit citizens can be selected as Supervisors
does not seem to be in the nature of a compliment.
The army has won nothing in esteem by glossing
over the offense of two officers at Honolulu who,
while drunk, placed themselves on exhibition. It is
not even probable that the army can square itself by
being severe with privates who are similarly obstrep
erous.
Mayor Phelan laughs at a practice for which Stern
berg went to San Quentin for a term of years, al
though at the time he said the offender ought to have
a life sentence. The difference is that now the of
fender is Phelan.
According to the Mayor there are four wards in
this city which cannot furnish anybody fit to repre
sent them in the Board of Supervisors. There must
be a better excuse than this for "colonization."
One boy has shot another at Quincy, as usual em
ploying an empty rifle for the purpose. The Coroner's
jury returned a verdict of accidental death, but the
death was not accidental. It was criminal.
Since the Examiner has its spy system in opera
tion, it might keep track of Phelan as well as of Pat
ton. The public is really interested in the way the
Mayor spends his time.
Spanish officials seem to be sorry that they yielded,
but the time is too late for any but vain regrets. How
ever, if they want an opportunity to yield again, in all
likelihood they can get it.
PURITY OF THE PHELfIN STONDORD.
\yi ANY people have believed that the Mayor,
/ \ whatever his fault of arrogance, regardless of
* * his absurd assumption of power, was guided
by an honorable intent. He has prated of purity in
politics, and they have blindly overlooked base
methods to which he has resorted. They could not
think he would be capable of false pretense. They
chose to consider him as having been maligned.
To The Call has fallen the task of unmasking
Phelan. It was not a pleasant task. Neither was it
one to be evaded. It has found the purified and
purifying Mayor guilty of practices for which men of
less influence, unequipped for doing as much harm,
have been sent to prison and stripes, and it has told
the story. When Sternberg went to San Quentin
for the crime of colonizing voters, one who most
roundly condemned him was Phelan. When he was
pardoned, after having served two-thirds of his term,
the ground being that he had been the tool of others,
who had wholly escaped, it was the Mayor who most
loudly voiced a holy indignation, declaring that
Sternberg or anybody similarly reprehensible merited
a life term.
It seems to us that the act of the Mayor in in
ducing Eugene Murphy, an honorable citizen, to
"colonize" in a ward other than that of his residence,
was a greater crime than that perpetrated by Stern
berg. At least the hollow hypocrisy of it gives it a
worse look. Sternberg did not stand forth as a re
former. He was not the talking apostle of nobler
and loftier politics. He was not a man of wealth and
standing. He had not been trusted by the people with
a great office. Phelan did not colonize a mere voter,
but it was his design to have Murphy elected a Su
pervisor. He made a specious plea that the course
was legal, in the interest of good governmentâ was,
indeed, laudable* So well did he present the case
that for the nonce he convinced Mr. Murphy that no
evil was being done.
But to the colonist there came a second and wiser
thought. He reflected that the course was morally,
even if not legally, wrong, and withdrew. He went
back to his home and left the Mayor to handle the
purity business without him. Mr. Murphy is an up
right citizen, his word acceptable wherever he is
known. Moreover, it is in this instance enforced by
circumstantial evidence. There is no escape for the
Mayor from the conclusion that he has been caught
in a deliberate attempt not only to colonize a voter,
but a candidate. If Sternberg merited a life term,
what shall it be said Phelan merits?
The Mayor is backed by the unclean Examiner
and the equally nasty Bulletin. Possibly he could
have officially survived the handicap of such friend
ship as this, but when he is exposed as a trickster,
his insincerity made plain, his virtuous words robbed
of their effect, his pious attitude shown to be a cant
ing pose, it may be said that he has brought about
his own defeat. Neither the throwing of mud nor
money can save him now.
PHELfIN'S WATER RATE DIVERSION.
THE case of Fitch vs. the Board of Supervisors,
which filled so large a space in the public eye
about this time last year, having been decided
by the Supreme Court in favor of the officials whom
Mayor Phelan ousted from office, it will be interesting
to observe the comment of our reform Democratic
fellow citizens upon the reasoning of the Judges.
An evening contemporary remarks that the scheme to
oust the Supervisors for failing to fix water rates in
February, 1897, was an attempt to Mexicanize the city
government. By this it is intimated that the Mayor
endeavored to change the local government without
resorting to the formality of an election.
If it be true, as alleged, that the Mayor actually did
contemplate Mexicanizing the city, he must have been
acting under the advice of his esteemed Mexican fel
low citizen, Barclay Henley, now his candidate for
District Attorney. Mr. Herrley, as is well known, was
formerly a Mexican Marquis, and is therefore prob
ably familiar with the Mexican method of overturn
ing governments.
The decision of the Supreme Court, however, will
not be likely to promote the political aspirations of
Mayor Phelan. On the contrary, those who take the
trouble to read it will perceive that between the lines
it administers a deadly blow to the species of dema
gogy upon which he has been making politics in
this city, and by means of which he has hoped to lift
himself into the Lord-Mayoralty under the new char
ter. The court declares that proceedings for ousting
officials for dereliction of duty under our law must
be criminal in their nature, that no crime was com
mitted by the Supervisors in making a water rate in
vestigation which ran over the month of February,
and that if a valid water ordinance can be passed in
any other month â which the court has already decided
could be done â when the constitution declares that
rates shall be "fixed" in February it means that pro
ceedings shall be commenced in that month.
The monstrous feature of Mayor Phelan's theory of
fixing water rates under the constitution and the act
of 1881, however, receives little attention from the
court, perhaps because it was not necessary to con
sider it. Reference is made to his idea that innocent
Supervisors must be punished along with the guilty
for malfeasance in office. It will be remembered that
Lackmann and Rottanzi opposed laying over the or
dinance of 1897. They protested against Dr. Clin
ton's motion to postpone and declared that if adopted
it might endanger their official tenure. Yet when the
proceedings to oust the board came along they were
included among the guilty and Judge Wallace held
that they must also be punished by being deprived of
their offices.
Subsequently the Mayor reappointed them, but the
precedent he sought to establish was clear. This pre
cedent was that innocent men must be punished in
order that the guilty might be reached â a principle
so abhorrent to all justice as to approach perilously
near barbarism. The purpose of Phelan and Fitch
in adopting this theory was, however, ill-concealed.
They were. forced to save Supervisors Clinton and
Dodge, who had been chiefly instrumental in post
poning the fixing of water rates, and they ousted the
board as an entirety to do it. Then Phelan rose to
the occasion by appointing the four anti-Spring Val
ley Supervisors to his new board.
Now that the Mayor and his assistants in this
scheme to obtain possession of the government by
force are on record as a pack of fools, the people
should not fail on November 8 to express their
opinion of all of them who are running for office.
The Rev. Mr. Nead w/is not convicted of vagrancy
because there was no evidence against him. Eleven
jurors out of twelve thought him innocent, and what
the other juror thought is a matter for speculation.
When Mayor Phelan is detected in an act which
seems contrary to the spirit of the law he explains
that it is in the interest of good government.
Phelan has had one Board of Supervisors. He is
trying to pick another. There is no reason for
thinking his judgment has improved.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 19, 1898.
A COftIBINfITION OF SLANDERERS'
A COMBINATION made up of Maguire, the
Democratic State Committee, the Examiner
and certain anonymous persons who clearly
represent the notorious school book ring has begun
a campaign of slander against State Printer A. J.
Johnston, who has been renominated by the Republi
can party and who will be supported by all citizens
whose minds are too honest to be affected by slander
and who desire an efficient administration of the
office.
Fortunately for Mr. Johnston, the record of his ser
vice has been made public by an official investigation.
The slanders put forth against him by the writers of
an anonymous circular in the interests of the school
book ring, and supported by Maguire, hi 9 henchmen
on the Democratic State Committee and his organ,
the Examiner, have all been thoroughly inquired into
and disproved. The refutation of the slander is to be
found in the official records of the State.
When the charges were originally made they came
almost directly from the school book ring. The last
Legislature appointed a committee to investigate
them. That committee was composed of three mem
ber*, of each House, and represented both political
parties. For the better performance of its work the
committee appointed four experts â two Republicans
and two Democrats â to examine the work and the
accounts of the office. Two of the experts were
skilled accountants, and two were experienced in the
printing and bookbinding trade. Their report was
unanimous, and concludes as follows:
"The testimony taken has been very full, and much
time has been devoted to this hearing. And, having
given the whole subject careful consideration, we are
of the opinion that the office of Superintendent of
State Printing has been conducted on business prin
ciples and with economy and due regard for the pub
lic interests."
The object of the school book ring in making the
fight against Mr. Johnston is to brfeak down the State
school book system and leave the schools and the
people of California once more a prey to their plun
dering. Our own experience taught us what that
ring wa* in times past, and the reports that come from
the East at every session of the State Legislatures
there show it is even worse now in its greed and in
its corrupting power than it was in the days when
California was one of its victims.
It is significant of the cunning and the trickery of
the ring that it has not made an open attack upon
Mr. Johnston. It has prepared an anonymous cir
cular and arranged to get Maguire's State Committee
to circulate it and indorse it. The circular was sent
out by the committee accompanied by a letter signed
by Seth Mann, chairman, indorsing it and requesting
the Democratic press to copy it. Notwithstanding
this indorsement by Seth Mann, the Examiner said
yesterday: "Concerning the authenticity and author
ity of the original circular we are unable to speak, for
we do not know who is responsible for its appear
ance." If the Examiner will inquire of Mr. Mann
it will probably find that Maguire is responsible for
its appearance, and that back of Maguire is the
school book ring.
The animus of the whole thing is revealed in the
sneers of the Examiner at the State School Book
League. That organization, representing California
labor and California schools and having no other ob
ject than that of protecting our people from the
rapacity of a ring whose corruptions are notorious in
every State in the Union, is recognized to be, like the
State Printer, a dangerous foe to the ring. Hence
the combination.
Meantime Maguire poses as a friend of labor.
Meantime the Democratic State Committee claims to
be opposed to corrupt rings and monopolies. Mean
time the Examiner protests it does not know who is
responsible for the anonymous slander.
\A ISS GENEVIEVE RANSOM of Nebraska
/ \ is to be congratulated. She got as far as the
* * altar with a young man, and there he left her.
Of course she fainted, for the surprise was consider
able, and the wedding feast had been prepared re
gardless. The almost-groom balked because he de
tected on the breath of the pretty-near-bride the faint
and yet significant odor of a cigarette. He turned
upon her, spoke the accusing words and then turned
upon his heel and went away from there.
Fortunately she did not attempt any defense. Per
haps had she pleaded tearfully he would have re
lented. Possibly if her big brother had given him a
kick he would have braced up. In which case. Gene
vie ye, instead of being free and at liberty to smoke
cigarettes, would have been tied until death or divorce
to a* tyrant. Undoubtedly the deserter has all the ele
ments of a domestic boss. He would have ruled the
house with a rod of iron and probably placed his wife
on an allowance of one print dress a year. Now she
can pucker her rosy lips around as many cigarettes
as she pleases.
It seems that she had promised to cease the prac
tice, but just an hour before the time set for the cere
mony had been induced by a bridesmaid to take a
farewell whiff. In swearing off any habit the reformer
is accorded the privilege of tapering. There is no
wisdom in giving the sensibilities a violent wrench
when they can be trained to new ways by pleasant
degrees. The surprise in the whole matter is not so
much the conduct of the groom, who must be reck
oned a crank, but the fact that Nebraska girls indulge
in cigarettes. There has been an idea that this habit
among American women was confined to cities, but
never that the pastoral stretches of the Middle West
contained yellow-fingered bachelor maids with
breaths redolent not of the wholesome onion, but of
tobacco. Nevertheless, while in no manner lending
approval to the course of the bachelor maids, they
have a right to follow it if they choose, and any man
to be scared from the altar by it does not constitute a
grave loss.
According to the Examiner the present Board of
Supervisors is a "vile crew." Perhaps the allegation
gives little ground for dispute, but it must be re
membered that this same crew was selected by Mr.
Phelan.
As all of the bad Indians but have agreed to
surrender there is hardly a probability of a general
war. The troops available ought, with the raw mate
rial, to be able to make the solitary renegade good.
Vanderbilt remarked that the public might be
damned. Phelan echoes the remark, and an unrea
sonable public objects, for times have changed.
Nobody can blame the Mayor for laughing when
charged with illegal acts. In fact his only chance to
laugh is between now and November 8.
Misled by an exaggerated idea of his own impor
tance, Phelan says, "The King can do no wrong."
Only Phelan isn't king.
When Dewey shall reach this city the people will be
too busy welcoming him to inquire as to his politics.

HOW TO COUNT
PUBLIC FUNDS.
The class In city politics was in session
and the master asked a number of ques
tions that were pertinent and of decided
importance to the voters of this city at
this time.
Define president of the Board of Super
visors in San Francisco.
The Mayor, elected by the city and
county at large, is the president of the
Board of Supervisors, and it is his duty
to preside at all sessions of the board
without the right to vote.
What are the specific duties of the pres
ident of the Board of Supervisors of San
Francisco?
The law says: "H shall be the duty of
the president of the Board of Supervisors
vigilantly to observe the uffiqial conduct
of each Supervisor in his district, and of
all public officers of the city and county,
and take note of the fidelity and exacti
tude, or the want thereof, with which
they execute their duties and obligations,
especially in the collection, custody, ad
ministration and disbursement of the pub
lic funds and property."
What must he do in the carrying out of
his specific dutlp*?

Mr. Shadburne thanked him for what he had

paid It was better to delay action regarding
Bacon place until after election, but that he

of him; that It was his duty to see to the in

No matter how capable and well-intentioned

because we discovered that an ordinance was

culiar. It was discussed at a meeting of the

What was his purpose in inserting the

ing: the handling and custody of public

est, and see thnt he was not tempted.

quired by law and saw the money with
his own eyes, no gold bricks or iron to
kens in bags could be foisted on him
for genuine money, and he knew that- if
the Treasurer or other official had to show
once a month the amount of coin his hooka
called for as a balance he could not
have an opportunity to play the flim-flam
game andTipproprlate the people's money.
Who is the president of the Board of
Supervisors of San Francisco?
James D. Phelan.
Did he do his duty as such officer as re
quired by the law?
In one matter he did not. He willfully
neglected to SEE AND CO I 'NT themoney
in the hands of the City and County
Treasurer, and allowed him to play the
flim-flam game and at the same* time
cast a stain on the fame of th^ city
by proclaiming to the world that it
had a dishonest official.
Can a man who will neglect a plain
duty of the highest importance be
trusted in the administration of public
affairs?
A man who can be false to his trust in
one matter can be false in every matter
and therefore should not be trusted with
further opportunity to mismanage pub
lic affairs. E. C. S.
Sutro Railroad Assessment.
An order was made by Judge Coffey di
recting Mrs. Emma Merritt and W. H.
R. Adamson. executors of the will of the
late Adolph Sutro, to pay an assessment
of |2 a share on 21.310 shares of the cap-,
ltal stock of the Sutro Railroad Com
pany, owned by the estate of the dead
capitalist. It was shown that through
the anxiety of creditors of the road to
enforce payment of claims it was neces
sary that the assessment should be paid.

WHAT IS
CREAM OF TARTAR?
It comes from grapes and is yielded in
the process of wine-making, most
extensively in France and Italy.
Cream of tartar is brought in a crude state to this
country, where three-quarters of the entire importation is
refined to the highest grade in the works of the Royal
Baking Powder Company.
' This highly refined, pure cream of tartar â the product
of the healthful grape â is the material from which a
pure and healthful baking powder must be made. More
expensive, of course, than other ingredients frequently
used, but being healthful itself it adds healthfulness to
the food instead of degrading it as do the baking powders
that are degraded in quality to meet a degraded price.
The celebrated Royal Baking Powder is made of
highly refined, pure and healthful cream of tartar. Its
great qualities have made it famous in every country in
the world, and it is the most appreciated of all baking
preparations by all good cooks and up-to-date house-
keepers.

DRINK DROVE HIM FRANTIC.
R. T. Owen, a Sewing Machine Agent,
Arrested for Assault to Mur
der a Bartender.
R. T. Owen, a sewing machine agent,
was arrested yesterday morning on a
charge of assault to murder. The com
plaining witness is Alfred Babie, a bar
tender at 527 Clay street.
Both men live in the Denver House on
Third street, near Howard. Between 7
and S o'clock yesterday morning while
Babie was walking along: Third street,
Just after leaving the Denver House,
Owen came up to him and without say
ing a word stabbed him in the left shoul
der and back with a knife.
Babie went to police headquarters to
report the affair, and was followed by
Owen, who was placed under arrest by
Policemen Isaacs and Morton. Babie
went to the Receiving Hospital, whero
his wounds were attended by Dr. Rum
well. Babie said he never saw Owen
before and could not conceive why he
should have attempted to kill him.
Owen had the appearance of a man who
was in an Incipient stuere of delirium
trtinens. He told an incoherent story of
Babie presenting a revolver at him on thÂ«
stairs of the Denver House and threat
ening to shoot him.
A SPIRITED AND
EFFECTIVE CAMPAIGN
HENRY S. MARTIN IS A MOST
POPULAR CANDIDATE.
His Peculiar Fitness for the Office
Generally Acknowledged.
Amons the candidates on the Repub
lican-municipal ticket there is not one
who is making a more spirited and ef
fective campaign than Henry S. Mar
tin, the nominee for Sheriff. That he
will be successful by a large majority
is a foregone conclusion. Personally
Mr. Martin is and always has been a
very popular gentleman. His genial
personality has won for him hosts of
sincere friends during his life-long resi
dence in this city, and these friends
will be glad of the opportunity of prov
ing their friendship by casting their
ballots on election day for plain, every
day Henry Martin.
Mr. Martin has been identified for
years with many benevolent anc l char
itable institutions, notably the French
Hospital. He is president of this in
stitution, and during the encampment
of the troops at Camp Merritt he was
among the prime movers in the prop
osition to give to sick soldiers the use
of the hospital gratis.
Mr. Martin's substantial property In
terests in this city make him peculiar
ly alive to the responsibilities of the
office he is seeking, while his extensive
business experience makes him emi
nently qualified for the discharge of its
duties in a highly satisfactory man
ner.
With candidates in general this is the
tlmf; when the "glad hand" is freely
offered, but in many cases, after elec
tion, the voter receives instead the i
"glassy eye." It is not so, however, !
in Mr. Martin's case. He has always i
been noted for his affability, and has ;
always been easy to approach at all
times and under all circumstances. As
voters find this gentleman during his
campaign, so have they found him be
fore and so will they find him after his
election, plain and unassuming, yet al
ways courteous and obliging; a man in
every respect deserving of the confi
dence, esteem and suffrages of his fel-
low men.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 19, 189 S.
GAS VETO IS
LAID OVER FOR
THREE WEEKS
No More Political
Capital In It.
ROTTANZI WANTS SOME LIGHT
THE "SOLID EIGHT" BECEIVE A
SHOCK, BUT RALLY.
A Canadian Lady Faces the Supervis
ors and Gets Her Pet Dog
Cheaply Released From
the Pound.
A special session of the Board of Su
pervisors was held yesterday afternoon
on the call of Mayor Phelan. The only
absentee was Supervisor Morton, who
was out of the city. It was quite evident
that the gas veto recently sent in by the
Mayor was the inciting cause for this
gathering of forces, as it was scheduled
to come before the board for final action.
No time was lost In reaching it. The
veto was read, declaring that the San
Francisco Gas and Electric Light Com
pany was not entitled to the payment of
Its street lighting bills for August and
September, aggregating about $15,000, as
there was no legal contract In existence.
Supervisor Smith at once moved to
postpone consideration of the veto for
three weeks. "That â will carry It past
election time," explained the Supervisor.
"I understand that some people ure try-
Ing to make political capital out of the
gas contract, and I object to such unfair
procedure."
Supervisor Rottanzl, who had voted to
overthrow the Mayor' 6 first veto of the
gas bills, took the floor and announced
that as the second veto was apparently
based upon more tangible grounds than
the first had been he was in favor of
having the City and County Attorney
render an opinion on the legality of the
gas contract and on the special objec
tions that the Mayor raised 10 it. "In
case that this legal opinion is favorable
to the Mayor's veto I shall probably vote
to sustain it," remarked Rottanzi. This
statement produced something of a sen
sation among the other Supervisors, as
it was by Rottanzl's vote last time that
the veto was overridden.
Sheehan wanted to have the veto laid
over for three weeks from next Monday.
By that time Supervisor Morton will be
home, and his vote is counted on against
the veto. Lackmann, Clinton and Dodge
advocated Immediate action on the veto.
Finally after several ballots Sheehan,
Britt, Devarry, Hasklna. Rivers, Delany
and Smith carried the day. and the veto
was laid over for three weeks.
Scarcely had this matter been disposed
of before Supervisor Clinton sprang a
surprise upon the entire board by stat
ing that a lady was present who had a
grievance against the Poundmaster wh!ch
she wished to explain to all of the Super
visors. He asked the vcourtesy of the
board to allow the lady a few moments.
Haskins was on his feet to protest, but
seeing no signs of objection on the part
of his confreres, he subsided with a pro
test that "such matters ought to go be
fore the proper committee."
A moment later Dr. Clinton brought
forward a very pleasant looking woman,
of rettned manners, " who spoke cleurly
and to the point. "I wish simply to
state," said she, "that yesterday after
noon my fancy pet dog was out in front
of the house near the door, when a rep
resentative of the poundmaster came
along, seized the animal and carried him
away. As the dog was a valuable one I
at once set to work to secure its release.
Some one told me that if I went to a
Supervisor and secured an order I could
get the animal out by paying 60 cents,
whereas if I simply went to the pound
without an order, it would cost me $2 60
to secure my pet's release. As I am a
Canadian, recently arrived in San Fran
cisco, I supposed that the advice was cor
rect and I went to Supervisor Clinton,
who happened to be the most easily
reached. I had never met him before,
but he very kindly gave me an order on
the poundkeeper, made out on a regular
printed blank, asking for the release of
the dog. Armed with this document I
went to the pound, where I was met by a
deputy, to whom I presented my paper.
He glanced at it for a moment and then
handed it back to me with the remark,
'It don't go. We don't recognize any per
mits hero that are signed by Clinton.
Only permits signed by the solid eight
are received.' Now, I don't know any
thing about the solid eight and I don't
see how I am to get the dog, unless I pay
the $2 60, or the Supervisors order - the
poundmaster to honor Dr. Clinton's per
mit."
The eight looked at each other In si
lence, not knowing whaft to say, while the
audience in the Supervisors' chamber
laughed aloud. Finally Brltt arose with
the inquiry, "Are you sure that he said
'solid eight?' "
"Quite sure , sir; those were his very
words," responded Mrs. Simpson.
"Then come over here, madam," ex
claimed Captain Delany, "and I will
make it all right," and escorted by Dr.
Clinton, Mrs. Simpson crossed the room
to the captain's seat and secured his sig
nature below that of Clinton's, request
ing the poundmaster to liberate the dog.
"That will go, ma'am," explained the
captain, "but I wish you to understand
that this thing of liberating animals
from the pound on a Supervisor's request
is not obligatory upon the poundmaster.
It is only a favor."
With this the scene came to a close,
with suppressed comment throughout tne
The board appointed Alfred Fuhrman
collector of delinquent taxes. Edward W.
Williams was reappointed Justices' Clerk
for a period of two years. An order was
passed prohibiting the sale of theater
tickets by any person not holding a li
cense, the rate for a license being fixed
at $25 per quarter or JIOO per yenr.
A leave of absence for forty days was
granted to Park Commissioner A. i>.
Spreckels.
The board killed the resolution prohib
iting bicycles and vehicles from approach
ing within five feet of a street car when
stopping to take on or let off passengers.
The Bohemian Amateur Athletic Club
was granted a license to hold an amateur
boxing exhibition at B'ne B'rith Hall.
October 24. The Athletes' Club was given
permission to hold a masquerade ball in
Elntracht Hall. October 22.
The clerk was Instructed to advertise
for proposals for three lire engines, a
hook and ladder truck and a chemical
tank, to be paid out of the new Insurance
Fund. ________â____â.

paper at Clark's. 853^ Market street â¢
MRS. RICHARD BURKE DEAD.
Cablegram From Ireland Announces
Her Demise.
Michael Meagher has received a cable
message announcing the death of his
niece, Mrs. Richard Burke, at Fethard,
Tipperary, Ireland.
Mrs. Burke was born In this city In
1860. She was a daughter of James Dona
hue, who with his brothers, Michael and
Peter, established the Union Foundry in
this city In 1849. He was, until the time
of his death in 1862, the president of the
San Francisco Gas Company.
The late Colonel Peter Donahue, who
built the San Francisco and North Pacific
railroad, was Mrs. Burkes uncle. Her
brother, Peter J. Donahue of Laurelwooa
Farm, Santa Clara County, is now trav
eling in Europe.
Mrs. Burke, whose maiden name was
Margaret Donahue, was married at St.
Mary's Cathedral in this city to Richard
Burke, barrister at law, of Clonmel, Ire
land, in ISS2. Their married life has been
a most happy one. She leaves a husband
and four children to mourn her loss.
Mrs. Burke was known to a large circle
of friends in this city, who will mourn
her loss. She was noted above all things
for great gentleness of character and her
never falling Bymjpathy for the poor.
WHERE THOSE
MEDICOS TAN
HUMAN SKIN
Mr. Peterson's Candid
Admission.
HE USES SALT AND ALUM
DR. TUGCH.ES OF TOLAND'S IS
VERY NON-COMMITTAL.
He Denounces the Practice as Based
on Theft, and Hopes The Coll
Will Succeed in Stamp
ing It Out.
The expose of the unlawful use of hu
man skin purloined from autopsy and
dissecting rooms has caused widespread
fear in the breasts of the students of the
colleges. The professors and higher med
tcal men universally denounce the prac
tice of tanning the human skin. Any
student convicted of the crime will be ex
pelled from college.
The demonstrator of anatomy of Toland
Medical College, Dr. Samuel G. Tuggles,
when interviewed yesterday, could not
deny the truth of the statements of The
Call, and said:
"What stand the university may take
in the matter I cannot say. For my own
department I can say that I do not know
any particular student who may have
tanned or caused to be tanned human,
skin. The subjects are skinned before
being dissected. I am not always in the
dissecting room, and I suppose some of
the skin gets tanned. The Call seems to
know more about that than I do. I have
been very much Interested in what it had
to say, and I hope It will put a stop to
this disgusting practice.
"The students are obliged to attend the
lectures three times a week under the
different physicians who lecture at the
City and County Hospital. They do at
tend autopsies and probably have oppoK
tunities to remove the skin. It. is steal
ing- and against the rules of the college, !
and when a student is going to break a
rule he does not tell the professors be
forehand.
"Yea, 1 know that medical students do
such things, but If they do it at Toland
1 dt> not know anything about it."
There is a tannery at :Ul2 Army street,
Just back of thi? Cogswell College, run by
Poetsch & Peterson. A physician gave
this place as the one which does the tan
ning for the young physicians connected
with the City and County Hospital.
At the tannery Mr. Peterson described
again the details of the business as revolt
ing as the smells which live around his
place. He F;<id:
"I have tanned plenty of human skin
for the medical students and dot-tors who
cut up the people to see what killed them
before they died."
"You are sure you can do it well; I am
very particular."
"Oh. yes: I have been doing It for two
or three years. It is not a hard thing
to do. I just soak it one day in strong
salt and waterâ plenty of salt. Then,
when it gets pretty soft I soak it for
aluiut a week in some alum and salt and
water. Then if it must be dark I smoka
it."
"I see. Can you show me a piece?"
"I have one little piece, but ft is not
so good. You see it was poor skin to
begin with."
"Do you know where this skin came
from?"
"A doctor who was just cutting people
up at the county hospital. It was wrapped
up in a paper and was fresh."
"Did he say he was from the City and
County Hospital?"
"Oh, yes. They all Fay they come from
there. You see it is not so far away. Can
not I tan some for you? I can do line
work."
An old man, a Mr. Booth, who has
worked in the tannery and lives at Ilry
ant and Twenty-sixth streets, said he had
seen human skin in the office of Poetsch
& Peterson, and that they could tan it
because they did not use bark.
"Won't bark tan the skin?"
"Makes it as rough as a bear's back."
"Do they tan much skin?"
"Yes. They try to keep it secret-like,
but the doctors and young medical stu
dents bring in pieces to tan every little
time. They handle it in the office, but
the doctors don't makf any sreret of it.
They want the leather for slippers or
razor strops, or something like that."
"What do you think about this tanning
of human skins?"
'Poor folks can't afford to think of any
thing. "Taln't no use."
A shoemaker at Twenty-sixth and
Howard streets pays he has seen human
skin in the office of Poetsch & Peterson.
"The boss showed me a piece of skin
a few days ago and asked me if I knew
what It was. It looked just like pig skin,
but I knew it was tanned human skin,
bocaupe the pores are larger. He said
the doctors from the City and County
Hospital had brought It to him."
The internes of the City and County
Hospital are mostly young physicians,
and upon them rests the accusation of
the tanners. Over them are Dr. F. W.
Dudley. Dr. J. O. Hirshfolder, Dr. W.
W. Kerr, Dr. C. N. Ellin wood and Dr.
R. A. Mcl>oan. Yesterday afternoon the
only interne at the City and County Hos
pital who was visible was D. V. Lu
chetti, who Pays he has no Christian
name. He is a young mnn,- very young.
He has an idea, apparently that the au
topsy room as well as the entire hospital
was founded and Is maintained for the
benefit of young doctors and students.
He said: "Why, I never saw any skin
Jn the autopsy rooms, did you? Do you
want to have some tanned? I do not
know anything. So Mr. Peterson says we
give him skin to tan. I can't say. I have
all of mine yet. Come down town and
lett have a good talk over the matter.
I never have a word to say to such peo
ple as Peterson; my time is too valua
ble. I just go to the autopsies and see
what I want to see, but never anything
else. See?"
"But, Dr. Luchetti. this is not a joke."
"Is it not, though?"
EMEBIC CONTEST DISMISSED.
An Action in Equity Now Relied
Upon to Determine Title to the
Property in Dispute.
The contest and amended contest of
Harry Emeiic to the- application for the
probate of the will of his late wife, Eu
genic Emerlc, were dismissed yesterday
by stipulation. This ends the litigation
in the courts of Ban Francisco, but a suit
in Contra Costa County yet remains to be
disposed of.
By the dismissal filed yesterday Mr.
Emerlc relinquishes his claim to Borne
$10 000 worth of jewelry bequeathed to
Lorena Myrtle Barbier by the will of Mrs.
Emeric. Mr. Erneric will now concen
trate his efforts in an endeavor to estab
lish hiÃ contention that the Contra Costa
property, valued at between $tW,OOO and
$80 000 was held in trust by Mrs. Emeric
and consequently she had no power to
dispose of it by testament. Emeric al
leges that several years apro, when as
he supposed, he was on his death bed.
he deeded the property to his wife to
hold in trust, the trust to be dissolved In :
event of his recovery.
When he recovered his wife refused to
suraln place the property in his hands, and
ifter her death It was found that she had
beaueathed it to her sister. Miss Barbier.
Mr Emeric now seeks to obtain Judg
ment of the Contra Costa court, declar
ng that the property was held by his
wife In trust and that Miss Barbier is
not entitled to the f state by reason of
the title vested by the will or her de
ceased "later.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Bllver unchanged.
Wheat and Wheat freights firmer.
Barky doing better.
Oats and Corn weaker. t
Rye dull.
Flour unchanged.
Beans and Seeds dull.
Hay very Quiet.
Potatoes and Onions unchanged.
Butter continues to decline.
Eggs and Cheese unchanged.
Poultry in free supply.
Game about the same.
Fruit market quiet.
Dried Peaches in rather better request.
Provisions slow and weak.
Hops in fair demand.
Hides quiet and Wool neglected.
Choice Beef and Mutton firm.
No change In Hogs.

NEW YORK DRIED FRUIT MARKET.
The New York Commercial, under date of
October 11, says:
"There la little perceptible difference as
compared with previous conditions. All dried
fruit men are willing- to admit that even
though prices are held firm, trade is dull and
demand much smaller than is usually expect
ed at this season.
"The raisin situation is not cleared at all.
Buyers are holding off, waiting for the an
nouncement of the association, and movement
Is restricted in consequence. There is gener- t
ally brisk tra<le in raisins at this season, but â ,
present circumstances prevent that, and the
entire trade has assumed a waiting attitude.
"Prunes are unchanged from last week.
Large sizes are wanted, but large sizes are
unobtainable. Buyers do not care to pay the
price aske<" for small sizes, and holders prefer
to keep their large ones to assist In moving
the undesirable stocks. The result is restric
tion of movement, which shows little Indica
tion of Increasing.
"Spot stocks of Valencia raisins are small,
â¢nd stocks to arrive are held at 6%e. There
Is some demand for old crop seeded, and prices
have advanced to G^c for 1-pound cartons, j
â while 3-crown are held firm at 7c. The first j
arrivals of new crop are expected about tha
middle of the week.
"The market is barely supplied with flgfc.
Estimates of yield are being reduced, and ]
from an early statement of 35.000 camel loads j
the figure is now down to about one-third that !
number, or one-sixth the yield for 1597.
"Apricots and peaches remain in virtually i
the same position as at last reports. Stocks !
are small; prices are ftrm; buyers and hold- !
ers appear to be apart in their views. The I
result is restricted trade and small movement, j
Some expert demand is noted, but no Import- j
ant sales reported."
WEATHER REPORT.
(120 th Meridianâ Pacific Time.)
PAN FRANCISCO, October 18, IS9B, 6 p. m.
The following maximum temperatures were re
ported from stations In California to-day:
Eureka SS. Fresno S4, Los Angeles S4, Red
Bluff 84, San Luis Obls-vo 92. San Diego 68,
Sacramento S2. Independence SO, Yuma 90.
San Francisco data: Maximum temperature
76, minimum 66, mean 66.
WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure is highest over Washington and
Oregon, and lowest In Southern Arizona. A
trough of low pressure extends from Arizona
northwestward through the great valleys of
California: The pressure has risen over the
Rocky Mountain and plateau regions, and fallen
along the Pacific Coast.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. October 19. IS9S:
Northern Californiaâ Fair, except partly
cloudy along the northern coast Wednesday;
cooler; fresh northerly wind.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday; cooler
In eastern portion; t'resh northerly wind.
Nevadaâ Fair Wednesday.
Utahâ Fair Wednesday.
Arizonaâ Fair and cooler Wednesday.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Fair Wednesday;
fresh, generally northwest wind.
Special report from Mount Tamalpaisâ Clear;
wind west, Â« miles; temperature 69, maximum
temperature 75. G. H. WILLSON.
Local Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. Oct. 18.â Prices of stocks
drifted sluggishly and to all appearances aim
lessly all through to-day's session. The almost
stagnant condition of the market was scarcely
relieved by a single feature. Even the "trust"
stocks fell into neglect after a long period of
fluctuations. Tobacco was forced down at the
opening on explicit announcements that the
much-talked-of plug tobacco combination had
once more fallen through. There was injected
Into the dullest portion of the day's tra<i:ng two
successive transactions of 12,500 shares and 6000
. shares respectively of Tobacco preferred at 115
"eeller 30. " This represented a transaction of
over $2,0u0,000 and was supposed to show the
disposal of tt.-a company's treasury stock, ap
plication for the listing of which was made
yesterday. The common stock was rather fever
ish for a time aftsr this, but settled down into
steadiness and neglect. There was an upturn
In sugar aIBO during the day, which was ex
plained by the news that certain grades of soft
sugars had been advanced in price. Realizing
subsequently wiped out practically all the ad
vance. The railroad list held generally firm
throughout the early part of the day, but sub
sequently eased off in sympathy with sugar and
the Northern Pacifies. The dally attack on the
coalers lacked vim and the decline only reached
a point in New Jersey Central. The traffic
statement of St. Paul for the second week in
October, showing an increase over last year' 3
earnings of nearly $140,000, is undoubtedly a
pretty fair indication of what is going on In
the grain-carrying roads. All reports agree
that it is difficult to secure enough cars to
meet the demand for the freight movement,
both east and west bound. The speculatlva
public apparently views these developments
â with apathy. The same apathy existed to-day
In all the security markets of the world, the
pause in London being attributed to waiting for
more settled political conditions and the com
pletion of liquidation In Berlin. The process of
accumulating exchange here is going on from
day to day and the banks continue to gain cash
from the sub-treasury. The final allotment of
Government 3s from Washington makes It

CHICAGO. Oct. 18.â An advance of l@l%d tn
wheat at Liverpool over night, the reported

accessible to Peking, In addition to reports

i loads of wheat engaged for export, stirred the

and lively affair of it. The stormy_ weather
In the Northwest was an additional influence.
It soon developed, however, that In the face
of thl6. Influential people here were selling
through brokers and commission firms. Then
came a denial of the export business at the
seaboard and there were reports also that the
j exporting firms were undeniably taking profits
jon long wheat. The actual export clearings
, wer* not up to the usual high total and the
primary market receipts Indicated that the
big export business could be supplied and leave
a surplus here besides. The early sales caused
a drifting down of the price and December,

DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
Choice Wine Grapes are scarce, and some
sold over the quotations.
Cantaloupes and Nutmegs are doing better,
as there are few on the market now. Water
melons are neglected.
Blackberries are no longer worth quoting.

Supplies of table Grapes are smaller, but the
market continues dull.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrogrraphlc
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, ia
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail-
Ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matter*
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, If any. Is published
In the morning papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired), U. S. N., in charge.

DOTTIE COOKE
GAVE HER ALL
TO CHILDREN
Big Annex Begun to
Fabiola.
NAMED AFTER ITS DONOR
EIGHTEEN LITTLE ONES WILL
BE CASED FOR.
On Her Death Bed Miss Cooke Willed
Her Moderate Fortune to
Enlarge the Insti
tution,
am
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Oct. 18.
Ground will be broken to-morrow for
the building of the "Dottle Gooke" Annex
to Fabiola Hospital. This annex is to bo
built partly with money that was the per
. sonal property of Miss Dottie Cooke, who
died last spring. During her lifetime,
which ended so soon. Miss Cooke was al
ways interested in children, and when she
died her private account amounted to
$3000. When conscious that death was not
far off, she requested that her money be
spent in some manner for the benefit of
little children. Her mother, Mrs. Har
riet Lee Cooke, after some consideration,
offered to build a children's annex to
Fabiola, and to endow it in such a man
ner that it will accommodate eighteen
children, which is one for each year of
her daughter s life.
The offer was formally made to the
lady managers of the Institution, and ac
cepted. The plans have bet-n prepared by
D. F. Oliver, the architect, and work will
be commenced right away.
The Dottie Cooke Annex will be two
stories In height and there will be one
large ward on each floor, with several pri
:ooms. A large playr. >om has been
arranged, which will be fitted with toy
and shelves and everything to make
the little unfortunates happy. The exte
rior of the building is of the Grecian style
of architecture and embodies all the latest
Improvements that have been developed in
similar Institutions. The connection with
the main building will be by an elevated
glass corridor from the second story.
A feature of the exterior will be the
porch entrance, which will be spanned by
a heavy arch supported on pillars. On the
face of this arch will be a tablet of stat
uary bronze, bearing the inscription.
"Dottle Cooke." The keystone will unfold
the words, "Her wish for children."
Prominent in the interior will be a mem
orial window, which will be placed at the
end of the playroom and will contain the
portrait of the donor, surrounded by a
Wreath of flowers with a ribbon, on the
flowing end of which will be written, "For
my sake let the little one 3 be tended."
The contract for the building has been
already signed and It will be ready for a i
dedication by Christmas.
MRS. LANGE AGAIN
LEAVES HER HOME
THE DESERTED HTTSBAJTC) IS
VERY GENEROUS.
Shields His "Wife and Takes All the
Blame, but Would Like to
Meet His Rival.
-
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Oct. 18.
Mrs. Ottn Lange did not stay long with
her husband after her return from the
elopement with Mr. Barclay. She has
gone again, and her destination is un
known to her husband. He does not know
whether she has returned to Barclay,
but he prophesies great trouble for Mr.
Barclay should he discover that the err
ing pair are once more together.
Although Mrs. Lange returned to Oak
land last Friday, she disappeared the next
day, and has not been seen in this city
since, although some letters hays been re
ceived from her. Last Saturday she wrote
to her husband from San Francisco and
told him she was not yet ready to return,
he was advised not to worry over her.
and informed that when all the troublo
has blown over she may come back.
Mr. Lange has been to San Francisco
and has done everything in his power to

John A. Logan Bill, a waiter, aged 19
years, who Is detained at the Receiving

Bell-Kelley, at 861 Harrison street, and
has for some time been employed as a

He says he was taken to the Receiving Hos
pital this afternoon under false pretenses.
He appears perfectly rational, yet admits

two years at Agnewa Asylum. His father

PROSECUTION CLOSES
IN MEADOR'S TRIAL
FOSTER TESTIFIES TO PRISON
ER'S PREMEDITATION.
Attorney Deuprey Says the Prosecu
tion Has Assisted the Defense,
Which Will Close To-
Morrow.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Oct 18.
The prosecution has closed in the case
of James Nathaniel Meadors, who is on
trial for the murder of Jonas Ury, a pri
vate of Company C, Eighth Regiment Cal
ifornia Volunteers. The trial was re
sumed in Judge Kllsworth's court this
morning when George Fouls testified.
But very little additional light was
thrown on the killing.
There was a long delay when the court
opened this afternoon, owing to the tardi
ness of Attorney Deuprey'a appearance,
and when Meador's counsel did appear an
other delay was occasioned through the
difficulty of locating H. G. Foster, who
when he was finally brought into court
proved a very damaging witness against
Meadors. His testimony was to the ef
fect thai Meadors, when first persuaded
to put up his knife, had said, referring to
Ury: "Never mina, I'll get you yet; and
you won't be the first one, either." Wit
ness, on cross-examination, said that he
had been to Piedmont baths with Mead
ors, but he denied that he had ever at
tempted to create opinions unfavorable to
the prisoner. During the afternoon Pri
vate Jarvis was recalled.
At the conclusion of Jarvis' testimony
Deputy District Attorney Harry Melvin
arose and caused a surprise by announc
ing that the prosecution closed. "But,"
said he, "Mr. Dtuprey and I have stip
ulated that when Dr. S. H. liuteau can
come he be permitted to testify as to
the location and nature of the wound
which caused Ury's death."
This was admitted by Attorney Deu
prey, who thereupon also caused a little
surprise by announcing that the defense
would probably close by to-morrow after
noon.
"I hope that there will be no misun
derstanding as to the prosecution clos
ing. I thought it would close to-mor
row, and under the circumstances have
not marshaled our witnesses together;
but I feel satisfied th>t the defense will
close its case to-morrow. The prosecu
tion has assisted us greatly in ascertain
ing what took place prior to the morn
ing of August 16. We might save time
by adjourning until to-morrow, and in the
meantime 1 will prepare my instructions."
Before the matter went over, however,
Attorney Deuprey called Deputy Sheriff
Smith to the witness stand. He wished
to bring out a conversation that he claims
took place between the deputy and wit
ness, H. G. Foster, in which the latter
seemed anxious to create an unfavorable
opinion as to Meadors. Deputy District
Attorney Melvin, however, stoutly ob
jected and hie objections were sustained.

An Unknown Berkeleylte Crushed
Under the Wheels.
OAKLAND, Oct. 18.â An unknown man
was run over and killed by the Berkeley
local Rt the Oakland Mole Bhortly after
6 o'clock this evening. The lower Portion
of the body was horribly mangled, but the
face la perfectly recognizable.
The deceased 1b presumably a resident
of Berkeley. He tried to board the train
while In motion and fell under the cars.
He waÂ» evidently a stevedore and had
on a brown duck jacket, lined with, a
heavy velvet collar. The shoes were par
ticularly heavy. In his pockets was found
a bottle of black witch hazel naive and 25
cents in coin, but not a paper or anything
that would lead to his identification. ,

Rev. Mr. Coyle Scores

THE W. C. T. U. IN SESSION
REPORTS SHOWING THE WORK
OF THE ORGANIZATION.
Mrs. Sturtevant Peet Gives an Able
Address â How to Make the
White Banner
Popular. .
BERKELET, Oct. 18.â For the nine
teenth time in its history, the Woman's
Christian Temperance Union of California
met In annual convention to-day. The
full number of expected delegates have
! now arrived, and when the State secre
tary called the roll this morning, it was
found that enough were present to almost
fill the First Presbyterian Church in
which the conference is being held. The
white ribbon is everywhere conspicuous.
The delegates cling to their symbol of
purity, and the rules of the convention
require it to be worn upon all occasions.
With the opening session this morning
began the discussions of reform In which
the union is engaged. The temperance
workers are full of enthusiasm, and they
Bhow it at every opportunity. They cheer
for woman's suffrage, for prison reform.
fur the abolition of saloons, for prohibi
tion, for social purity, and for the raanl
foid movements in behalf of which the
white ribbon stands.
Mrs. is. Sturtevant Peet, the California
president, called the conference to order
shortly before 10 o'clock. A committee on
credentials was appointed consisting of
Mrs. R. A. EmmonÃ, Mrs. D. J. Spencer
and William W. Bangs, and on resolu
tions, consisting of Mrs. E. G. Greene,
Mi.ss S. M. Severance, Mrs. F. Bennett,
Mrs. Hardy and Mrs. A. J. Wheeler.
The secretary's report showed that there
are now 126 societies in the State, having
altogether 3047 members. From the re
port of the State Treasurer it was learned
that these had contributed a total sum of
$4890 39 during the last year. An increase
in membership of over 400 had been added
since the last convention. Reports from
the State superintendent of different lines
of special work showed that Interest in
them is on the Increase.
In a paper on "How to Help the "Work
Individually" Mrs. S. Vnn Lennep told
the white-ribbonists that their small white
tanm-r should be worn on all possible oc
casions. She argued for the instruction
of all children in the principles of scien
tific temperance, and paid a glowing trib
ute to Mrs. Hunt of Pennsylvania as the
originator of this branch of study.
The feature of this afternoon's session

no wanton . vocal display. He

The same artistic spirit Is mani
fest In the singing of Mrs. and

art Is there; Miss Heinrich has

portioned and warm and rich; she sings with copious color, and her enun
