MAX HLIINRICH.
was the annual address of the president.
Mrs. Sturtevant-Peet said:
"It Is because of unnatural and irra
tional conditions tliat the Woman's
Christian Temperance Union exists. This
is the twenty-fifth anniversary of the
crusade with which the movement orig
inated. For twenty-five years we have
Btood for purity and prohibition, ignored
by the courts and ridiculed by politicians.
It Is true the world takes little heed ot
our efforts. Our labor has been for free
dom. We look upon evil as the sole and
only cause of evil; It is only lack of back
bone and moral courage that tolerates
the saloon and perpetuates Its misery.
"There is too little sympathy and sup
port for the workers of prohibition. Too
many people are disposed to palliate the
evil which it combats. People who have
nothing but convictions upon this subject
are traitors, for it is treason for them to
ignore their duty toward existing condi
tions. The world brands us as fanatics.
But if to promote purity, to help the
fallen, is fanaticism, may God make us
all fanatics. Public opinion lauds deeds
of charity, yet It brands as fanatics
those who try to remove and eradicate
the causes of all this poverty and vice
which make this charity necessary."
The president was followed by the Rev.
W. W. Scuddor of Alameda, who, In an
address upon "The Responsibility for the
Status of Temperance To-Day ," laid the
blame for intemperance at the doors of
the church. "The fault was," he said,
"that the church sanctioned saloon li
censes. Even ministers and respectable
members of their congregations fail in
this respect. The church itself Is the only
organization that can change this condi
tion of things. Yet the church seems In
different. It does not put forth its great
est effort. It alone should inaugurate so
cial righteousness, and leave the State to
support and enforce it."
The Rev. John A. Wilson, D. D., enter
tained the audience with many humorous
stories. He argued for concentration of
effort In temperance reform. But the
reverend gentleman did not believe that
the church was responsible for intemper
ance, because, he said, the church did
not originate the evil. He estimated that
intemperance kept at least 30,000 people
each year out of churches and sent ;tt
least 100 ministers to drunkard's graves.
The Rev. John Coyle, D. D.. of Berkeley
said some hard things about ministers
who drink. "Intemperate ministers com
promise the morals of their congrega
tions." he said, "and, as a stream never
rises higher than its source, so the re
ligious reeling of the parish never gets
above the same level as that of the min
ister in charge."
At to-morrow's session the officers for
the ensuing year will be elected, as well
as the delegates to the next national con
vention.
DEATH OF F. W. WICKER.
He Succumbs to the Effects of the
Shot Accidentally Inflicted
on Sunday.
ALAMEDA, Oct. 18.â Frederick W.
Wicker, who shot himself accidentally on
Sunday afternoon while cleaning a pistol
at hlB residence, corner of Grand street
and Enclnal avenue, died this morning
from the effects of the wound. He passed
a reasonably comfortable night, the phy
sician remaining with him moat of the
time. At 7 o'clock this morning, how
ever, there waa a change for the worse.
Hia pulse went to 160 and his tempera
ture to 102, while there were indications
of blood poisoning as well as pleuro
pneumonia. All the efforts of the physi
cians proved unavailing, however, and
the Injured man passed away about 10
o'clock. Deputy Coroner Fowler will
hold an inquest to-morrow morning at 9:30
o'clock. Decedent was a native of Bruns
wick, Germany, aged 50 years, and leaves
a widow. The time of the funeral has not
yet been decided upon.

A NEW TAX LEVY
GIVES SOME RELIEF
OAKLAND, Oct. 18.â The City Council met to-night and again took up
the delayed tax levy. For several days the Tax Collector's office door
has been decorated with a sign containing these words: "No tax bills
ready. Rate not yet tixed."
An ordinance was already prepared when the Council met, and It was
at once seen that it had the support of the same six votes that passed the
former ordinance vetoed by the Mayor. Messrs. Pringle. Cuvelller and
Girard attempted to make a few changes, but the weight of votes was
against them, and with an explanation that It being so late something
must be done, it was passed to print.
The new ordinance reduces the amount for dredging the lake from
$SO,OOO to $60,000 and raises several of the department allowances above
those in the vetoed ordinance. The free library fund Is such that one of
the reading rooms only may have to be closed. The High School will
be $3000 below last year's cost. The street fund is cut, but as the ordinance
taxing vehicles 13 now being enforced much income is expected from this
source. This ordinance will be amended so that the vehicle tax will go
into the street fund. The amount for the east and west side sewers la
cut from $38,500 to $17,000 and the police and flre funds will be below those
of last year. The new ordinance fixes the levy at $1 21.
Reliance Athletic Club this evening drew
a large attendance. In the eight-round
contest between George Gates of the En
c-lnul Recreation Club and Jack Dennis
of the Olympic Club, the former showed
to good advantage and was given the de
cision. The other eight-round contest, be
tween Phil Gretiie of the Presidio and
"Bud" Parker of New Orleans, proved a
walkover for the soldier boy, who
knocked the Southerner out in the third
round. The preliminaries were interest
ing. "Kid" Johnson and F. Arnold boxed
a clever four rounds, and in one of
the other preliminari.s Charles Tye made
a show of T. McCarthy.

PROCLAMATION.
STATE OP CALIFORNIA,
EXECUTIVE DEPARTMENT,
SACRAMENTO, July 30th, 1898.
WHEREAS, The Legislature of the State of
California, at Its thirty-second session begin-
ning on the fourth day of January, A. D. 1597,
two-thirds of all the members elected to each
Of the two houses of said Legislature Voting
In favor thereof, proposed the following de-
scribed amendments to the Constitution of the
State of California, to-wlt:
AMENDMENT NUMBER ONE.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
4L)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment to section
eighteen of article eleven of the Constitu-
tion, In relation to revenue and taxation, by
which It Is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Section 18. No county, city, town, township,
board of education or school district shall In-
cur any indebtedness or liability In any man-
ner or for any purpose exceeding In any year
the Income and revenue provided for it for
such year, without the assent of two-thirds
of the qualified electors thereof, voting at an
election to be held for that purpose, nor un-
less before, or at the time of Incurring such
Indebtedness, provision 6hall be made for the
collection of an annual tax sufficient to pay
the interest on such indebtedness as it falls
due, and also provision to constitute a sinking
fund for the payment of the principal thereof
on or before maturity, â¢ which shall not exceed
forty years from the time of contracting the
same; provided, however, that the City and
County of San Francisco may at any time pay
the unpaid claims with Interest thereon for
materials furnished to and work done for said
city and county during the forty-third and
forty-fourth fiscal years, out of the income and
revenue of any succeeding year or years; pro-
vided, that any and all claims for making, re-
pairing, altering or for any work done upon or
for any material furnished for any street, lane,
alley, court, place or sidewalk, or for the con-
struction of ar.y sewer or sewers in said city
and county are hereby excepted from the pro-
visions of this section; and in determining any
claim permitted to be paid by this section, no
statute of limitations shall apply In any man-
ner; and provided further, that the City of
Vallejo, in Solano County, may pay its exist-
ing indebtedness incurred in the construction
of its water works, whenever two-thirds of the
electors thereof voting at an election held for
that purpose shall so decide. Any indebted-
ness or liability Incurred contrary to this pro-
vision with the exception hereinbefore recited,
shall be void.
AMENDMENT NUMBER TWO.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
â¢.,'â â â¢ 10.)
A resolution proposing to the people of the
Etate of California an amendment to the Con-
stitution of the State, by adding a new sec-
tion to be known and designated as section
â even and one-half, article eleven thereof, pro-
viding for the framing by the inhabitants of
counties of local county government acts for
their own government. The said proposed new
section to read as follows:
Section 7H. The inhabitants of any county
may frame a county government act for their
own government, relating to the matters here-
inafter specified, and consistent with and sub-
ject to the Constitution and laws of this State,
by causing a board of fifteen freeholders, who
have been, for at least five years. qualified
electors of such county, to be elected by the
qualified electors of such county, at any gen-
eral or special election, whose duty it shall be
within ninety days after such election, to pre-
pare and propose a county government act for
such county, which shall be signed in dupli-
cate by the members of such board, or a ma-
jority of them, and returned, one copy thereof
to the Board of Supervisors or other legisla-
land of such a county and the other copy
county, which shall be signed In dupli-
by the members of such board, or a ma-
y of them, and returned, one copy thereof
ie Board of Supervisors or other leglsla-
body of such county, and the other copy
to be sent to the Recorder of Deeds of the
county. Such proposed county government act
shall then be published in two papers of gen-
eral clrculation in such county, or if there ba
not two such papers, then in one only, for at
least twenty days, and within not less than
thirty days after such publication It shall be
submitted to the qualified electors of such
county at a general or special election, . and if
a majority of such qualified electors voting
thereon shall ratify the same, it shall there-
after be submitted to the Legislature for its
rejection or approval, as a whole, without
power of alteration or amendment, i and if ap-
proved by a majority of the members elected
to each house, it shall be the county govern-
ment act of such county, and Bhall in such
caÃe become the organic law thereof and super-
sede any existing county government act, and
ail amendments thereof, and all special laws
inconsistent with such county government act.
A copy of such county government act, certi-
fied by the President of the Board of Super-
visors or other legislative body of such county,
and authenticated by the seal of such county,
setting forth the submission of such county
government act to the electors, and its ratifica-
tion by them, shall be made In duplicate and
deposited, one In the office of the Secretary of
State the other, after being recorded In the
office of the Recorder of Deeds in the county,
among the archives nf the county.
All courts shall take judicial notice thereof.
The county government act so ratified may be
emended, at Intervals of not less than two
years by proposals therefor, submitted by the
leelKlative authority of the county, to the
oualiflfd electors thereof, at a general or spe-
cial election held at least forty days after the
publication of such proposals for twenty days
in a newspaper of general circulation in such
county and ratified by at least three-fifths of
the Qualified electors voting thereon,, and ap-
croved by the Legislature as herein provided
for the approval of the county government act.
In submitting any such county government act
any alternative article or proposition may be
Â©resented for the choice of the voters, and may
be voted on separately without s prejudice to
Ot It er snall b<* competent In all county * govern-
ment acts framed under the authority given by
this section, to provide for the manner in
which the times at which, and the terms for
which' several township and county officers
other than Judges of the Superior Court, shall
he elected' or appointed: for their compensa-
tion" for the number of such officers, for the
consolidation or segregation of offices, for the
number of deputies that each officer shall have,
and for the compensation payable to each of
such deputies, for the manner In which, the
mp , in which, and the terms for which the
members of all boards of election shall be
elected or appointed and for the constitution,
regulation compensation and government of
such boards, and of their clerks and attaches;
also to prescribe the manner and method by
which all elections by the people shall be con-
ducted- and may In addition determine the
tests and conditions upon which electors, po-
litical parties and organizations may partici-
pate In any primary election.
'Whenever any county has, In the manner
and method herein pointed out. adopted any
county government act, and the same shall
have been approved by the Legislature as
R'oresaid the direction of sections four and
five of this article providing for the uniformity
'Â srstem of county governments throughout
the State, and 'likewise providing for the elec-
t on and appointment of officers, and the regu-
lation of their, compengatlon. shall not apply.
Bald county government act shall, as to any of
the matters herelnabove provided for and de-
clared by such county government act, not be
subject to any law or^amendment enacted by
the T,Â»elslature. except by amendment first
submitted to the electors and ratified in the
manner herelnabove set forth.

GBAPE BUYERS IN TBOUBLE.
Gambitz & Dolheguy Call a Meeting
of Creditors.
Gambitz & Dolheguy, the produce com
! mission merchants of 216 Washington
; street, have called a meeting of their
j creditors for Friday. The firm has been
purchasing grapes round Geyservllle, and
j owing to a combination of circumstances
i rinds it difficult to fulfill its obligations
immediately.
HEALDSBURG, Oct. 18.â Gambitz &
Dolheguy began their operations in
Cloverdale just before the season opened
by contracting for grapes at $8 and $9 de
livered on the cars, cash in ten days. They
made purchases from Windsor to Clover
dale, but the bulk of their grapes were
i secured at GeyHerville. Some of those who
! sold to them became uneasy at delayed
1 payments, and one leading grape-grower
jat Geyservllle Bent a representative to
' San Francisco to look Into the matter.
! The result of this precaution saved him,
! for he disposed of the balance of his crop
i to a Healdsburg winery.
I A representative of a grape-grower here
returned from San Francisco yesterday
after interviewing Gambitz & Dolheguy,
and will be present at a meeting of the
creditors of the firm at San Francisco
on Friday next. While there will be many
sufferers In this part of Sonoma County
from the operations of the firm, there are
those well informed in the grape and
wine industry who maintain that Gam
bitz & Dolheguy have been a blessing to
the grape-growers of the county on the
whole, as they started the price at a
jrnod figure, and it is maintained that
had they not come into the field grapes
would have sold at a much lower price
compared to the good price received. The
loss pus* Ined in a few localities is ln
elgniflcar.t. _
Exhorter Nead's Case.
The question as to whether J. M. Nead,
the Methodist exhorter charged with va
grancy, will be tried again will be deter
mined by Judge Conlan on Saturday. It
Is not likely that a new trial will be
ordered, from the fact that the Jury stood
eleven for acquittal. Nead received a
letter yesterday from Chandler Bros.,
merchants. Rockford, 111., inclosing a cer
tificate of good character for the years
he was in their employment as salesman,
during which he handled thousands of
dollars. _. I

I . O. O. F. GRAND
ENCAMPMENT
Annual Session Opens
at San Jose.
REPORTS VERY ENCOURAGING
J. W. HARRIS OF THIS CITY FOR
GRAND PATRIARCH.
Congratulatory Message to President
McKlnley â Exemplification of
Degrees, Followed by
a Banquet.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 18.â All the grand of
ficers and about 200 delegates were
present this morning at the opening of
the forty-fourth grand encampment of
the I. O. O. F. of California in Odd Fel
lows' Hall. Besides these there are
about one hundred Patriarchs Militant
in the city and about the same number
of ladies who accompanied the visitors.
The grand patriarch called atten
tion to the position of Past Chief
Patriarch Ernest Dunker, for many
years sentinel of the Grand En
campment. Dunker Is now an inmate
of the Odd Fellows' Home at Thermo
lito. He has some means and it is sug
gested that the Encampment appro
priate a sufficient additional sum to
purchase him a $1000 life membership.
It is suggested that the Encampment
give its moral and pecuniary indorse
men to the "golden jubilee" celebration
of the order in California next year.
Decisions, appeals and other matters
were also dealt with.
Grand Scribe Barnes' report gives the
number of encampments as ninety-one,
with a membership on December 31,
1897, of 5334 â a gain of seventy-one for
the year, which, in view of the sur
roundings and also of the fact that this
branch of the order In many other
jurisdictions has lost, 1b very gratify
ing and encouraging.
Grand Treasurer Lemont Bhows the
receipts of the year to be: General
fund, with balance of $691 91 on hand
October 1, 1897, $3541 61; disbursements,
$2896 72"; balance on hand October 1,
1898, $Â§44 89. Mileage fund, including
balance October 1, 1897, $4208 40; paid
mileage of Los Angeles session, 1897,
52623 75; balance In fund October 1, 3 898,
$1584 65. Net resources of the Grand
Encampment. $2382 80.
The grand representative's report
shows that there are 54 Grand Encamp
ments, 2656 subordinate bodies fend 128,
930 patriarchs; total assets of subordi
nate camps. $1,529,131 59. The assets
of California encampments are $156,
462 39, or more than one-tenth of the
entire assets of the national jurisdic
tion. Total relief paid by the patri
archal branch throughout the national
jurisdiction, $265,965 15; paid by Cali
fornia, $17,458 15. The new encamp
ments Instituted during the year were:
Pacheco Encampment No. 50, at Hollls
ter, San Benlto County; Redwood En
campment No. 67. at Fort Bragg. Men
docino County; Azusa Encampment No.
54, at Azusa, L, js Angeles County ,
The following past chief patriarchs
have died since the last session: An
drew McAleer of No. 1, C. H. Mead of
No. 1, Hugh McCoy of No. 6, William
J. Tllley of No. 26, J. L. Brown of No.
34, G. E. Johansen of No. 57, Jacob Von
devate of No. 58, and Richard Siddail of
No. 82.
The reports of the grand trustees,
committee on by-laws and finance com
mittee were adopted. Those of other
grand officers were referred to commit
tee:;.
The following resolution. Introduced
by Grand Representative Duran, was
ordered forwarded to President McKln
ley:
Resolved, That the Grand Encampment.
I. O. O. F., of California, In annual ses
sion In the city of San Jose, send greet
lng to William McKinley, President of
the United States, and the soldiers and
sailors of our army and navy, congratu
lating them upon the wonderful and
speedy victories achieved by their brav
ery in the lute- war with Spain. To the
sick and wounded brave boys our heart
felt sympathy is extended. May their re
covery be speedy and their restoration to
home and family assured.
This afternoon the following nomina
tions of officers of the Grand TSncamp
ment were made: Grand patriarch. J.
W. Harris of San Francisco: errand
high priest, Joseph Foster of San
Diego; grand senior warden. P. D.
Gummoe of Nevada City; grand scribe,
William H. Barnes (incumbent) of San
Francisco; grand treasurer. Georce \V.
Lemont (incumbent) of San Francisco;
grand Junior wardens, C. H. Wever of
Alameda, A. C. Bates of San Jose. W.
E. Dubols of San Francisco, J. E. Har
ris of San Jose, James Ashman of Los
Angeles; grand representative, O. W.
Erlewine of Sacramento; errand trus
tees, H. S. Winn, F. A. Week. W. S.
Potter, all of San Francisco. The only
contest is over the office of junior war
den and the other officers will be elected
by acclamation to-morrow afternoon.
An amendment has been proposed by
which a patriarch wishing to Join an
other encampment can do so without
taking a withdrawal card or without
losing his membership In his original
encampment in the event that the en
campment he makes application to
should reject him.
I. O. O. F. Hall is crowded this even
ing with visitors. The patriarchal de
gree is being exemplified by Occident
Encampment No. 42 of Sacramento, and
the golden rule degree exemplified by
Oriental Encampment No. 57 of San
Francisco. Both degree teams are noted
all over the coast. The Oriental degree
team is sixty strong, has the finest
paraphernalia in existence and is said
to present its work in a manner unex
celled on the coast. A banquet follows.
ARGUMENT IS CONCLUDED.
A Decision in the Botkin Extradition
Matter Will Be Handed Down
on Saturday.
Whether or not Mrs. Cordelia Botkin
will be extradited to Delaware to stand
trial for the alleged poisoning of Mrs.
Dunning and Mrs. Deane will be decided
next Saturday. When the hearing of ar
gument was resumed yesterday morning.
Attorney Prank MeGowan proceeded to
make the concluding argument on behalf
of the accused. Mr. MeGowan confined
his argument to a repetition of the theo
ries advanced by associate counsel, George
A. Knight. He also Insisted that the
"constructive presence" of the prisoner
in Delaware was all very well in theory
but did not exist in law.
Taken altogether the proceedings
amounted to little more than a reitera
tion of the proceedings before Governor
Budd a short time ago. In which counsel
for the accused had all the best of the
matter. When Mr. MeGowan concluded
his argument the case was submitted
and will be decided on Saturday.
STOCKTON, Oct. 18.â W. A. Botkin of
this city, husband of Mrs. Cordelia Bot
kin, is much chagrined over the action of
Governor Budd in signing extradition war
rant, after what he had previously stated
in the matter. "I do not Delieve that they
will get my wife back to Delaware," said
he to-day, "but if they decide to extra
dite her, she might just as well be hanged
at once, for feeling overrides ail justice
there in this case. The people of that
State want some one to pay the penalty
for the crime committed and whoever
I might be taken back there will be con
1 victed beyond a doubt." J
SLANDER SUIT
NEARLY ENDED
Attorney Jarman Makes
His Argument.
DEFENSE SUBMITS THE CASE
EVIDENTLY A TRAP TO SHUT
OUT DELMAS.
The Latter Wishes to Be Heard, but
Rea's Counsel Vigorously Pro
testâMatter Taken Under
Advisement.
Special Dispatch to ThÂ« Call.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 18.â The opening
argument In the Jarman-Rea slander
suit was made to-day by Attorney A.
H. Jarman, son of the plaintiff. He
occupied the entire day. The facts of
the case were gone over and Kea was
referred to as a "pure-minded, high
souled" person, Interested in the wel
fare of the city, who stopped at nothing
crooked to attain his end. Rea's testi
mony was reviewed and the boss" falla
cies pointed out. Jarman finished a few
minutes before adjournment.
Judge Kittredge then asked Attorneys
Morehouse and Burchard if they wished
to begin argument. The defendant's at
torneys stated that they would submit
their rase without argument.
Delmas then wished to make a sup
plemental opening argument. This was
objected to on the ground that plaint
iff's attorney had completed his open
ing statement. Delmas cited authority
to sustain him, and Judge Kittredge
took the matter under advisement until
the opening of court in the morning.
It was evidently a trap set by Rea's
attorneys, but Delmas refused to fall
into it. By refusing to answer Jar
man's argument Rea's counsel ex
pected to shut out argument by Del
mas. If the court allows Delmas to pro
ceed to-morrow defendant's attorneys
will probably want to respond. The
case will go to the Jury some time this
week. _^_

WTLLIE LYNCH'S ASSAILANT.
The Preliminary Examination of Po
liceman Kenville Continued
Until To-Morrow.
The preliminary examination of Police
man Frank L. Kenvllle, charged with as
sault to murder In throwing his club and
striking the boy Willie Lynch on the
head, fracturing: his skull, was continual
before Judge Mogan yesterday afternoon.
The witnesses examined were Dr. Hart
ley of the Receiving Hospital, Fred Mey
ers, Otto Lemeke, William Schonbien,
Fred Smith. Michael Riley, J. H. Healy,
Leater Goldberg, Henry T. Jones, P. A.
Clifford and William Adams. Dr. Hart
ley's evidence related to the naturo of the
boy's wound. The other witnesses did
not see the club thrown or see It strike
the boy.
Thomas E. liUdlUgton.a grlpman on the
Valencia-street line, was called for the
defense. He testified that he saw four
policemen running after the boys and
that rocks were thrown, his car being
struck twice.
The further hearing was continued un
til to-morrow morning at 11 o'clock.

Mrs. Miguel Esqueda Suffering From

OAKLAND CAN
CONTROL HER
WATER FRONT
The Southern Pacific Cannot Ob
struct the Building of the Mole
for the Proposed New Ferry.
The Recent Decision of the Supreme Court
Removes the Obstacles to Such Im
provements â W. R. Davis on the
Effect of the Judgment.
The Supreme Court of the United States
awards to the public of the State of California
all the navigable waters in the basin in the
estuary in front of Alameda and in front of Oak
land waterward from low-tide line. This gives
public control of every wharf, dock, slip or pier
ever to be used in developing the commerce of
the east side of the bay and the city of Oakland. â
Extract from an Interview with ex-Muyor W. R. Davis.
The Interest that has been created
among the progressive citizens acros3 the
bay, by the announcement of the Realty
Syndicate of their intention to establish
a competitive ferry system between San
Francisco and limeryville, has aroused a
large tribe of the genus obstructor, who
would feel deeply grieved if such an en
terprise should be successfully inaugu
rated.
Aside from their animadversions upon
the financial status of the corporation
that proposes to build this line, they have
a pet "hammer" that is being used in
dustriously on all possible occasions.
This is embodied in the allegation that
a competitive ferry cannot be. established
by reason of the alleged fact that tha
Oakland Water Front Company, other
wise known ;is the Southern Pacific, con
trols the wharf outlets to the bay.
The fact that the recent decision of the
Supreme Court of the United States prac
tically brings to Oakland and Alameda
freedom from the despotism of the Water
Front Company, and has opened up vast
possibilities, has been carefully lost sight
of by a large proportion of the people
over the way, who still believe they are
"bottled up " by Uncle Collls' once deadly
grip upon the water frontage of the mod
ern Athens.
This judgment of the court makes all
euch statements ad to the powers of the
Water Front Company nonsensical and
the sooner the people of Oakland aro con
vinced of the fact the better. Under this
Judgment on a retrial the city Is now
putting i:i evidence to secure still further
advantage over the once despotic corpo
ration by taking more water frontage
away from it in addition to the nine
tenths obtained through the former litiga
tion. The meat of the decision is the
fact th.it it assures public Instead of pri
vate control of the actual shipping front
age of Oakland and the eastern shore of
the bay for all time.
William Ft, Davis, senior counsel for Oak
land in the famous case, referred ytstor
day to the fact that the people or that
City do not roahze the importance of their
victory, and gave a concise resume of the
results of the decision, which will be of
particular interest at this time. He said:
"Horace \V. Carpentier and the Water
Front Company, his successor, claimed
for over forty years title to the Oak
land water front, which, as described in
the ordinances and deeds under which
they claimed, included twelve and a half
square miles of navigable waters and
chore strip up to ordinary- high tide line.
About nine-tenths of this area lay water
ward from ordinary low tide line. That
ancient claim extended under and in
cluded the whole bed of the estuary of
S;u: Antonio, the whole bed of the salt
water basin opposite Ea.st Oakland, and
the triangular area opposite Oakland and i
A!am.Â»dH. Into the bay to the depth of
twenty-five feet of water and along a
'ship channel' frontage seven miles long
In the bay.
"The Supreme Court in the recent de
cision decided that the title to about nine
tenths ot this tv hole area still remained
In the State, as it never had been grant
ed to thf: town of Oakland by the Y^.-giH
lature and therefore could not have been
granted by the town to Curpentier or the
Water Front Company. That conclusion
whs reached by holding that the words
'ship channel' meant low tide line, be
yond which the grant did not extend.
So the Water Front Company obtains
only the narrow, irregular strip common
ly called tide landâ really water lotsâly
ing between ordinary high and low tide
lines. This comprises about one-tenth of I
what was originally claimed by the com- |
pany.
"How does this leave tha city of Oak- I
SOLDIERS MATCHED
AGAINST COLLEGIANS
ME2J OF IOWA REGIMENT WILL
GO TO PALO AJLTO.
A Football Game on the Stanford
Campus Which Will Be Well
Attended by Blue Coats.
The campus at Palo Alto will present a
novel sight to-morrow afternoon when
the entire lowa regiment surrounds the
football field to shout for their team in
combat with the Stanford University
eleven. A football game has been ar
ranged between the soldier pig-skin chas
ers and the college men, and it will be
played on the university grounds.
A warm time is expected, as the blue
coats will bÂ« paid off to-day, and they
â¢will follow the team to the theater of
the gridiron warfare. So soon as it was
announced in camp that the lowa foot
ball aggregation intended to cross swords
with the cardinal cohorts the colonel of
the regiment was sought and an oral
petition made in behalf of the men in the
ranks for a day's outinr. The colonel
did not fancy the idea of being separated
from his soldiers for an entire day. but
he finally relented and gave a day's leave
to his regiment.
Special terms have been made \wlth the
Southern Pacific and the volunteers will
leave on an early train in the morning
for Palo Alto. Lunch will be served at
the university dining hall. The game,
which will begin late in the day, will be
the source of much noise on both sides,
especially from the soldier sections. The
Stanford team will be much stronger than
the eleven put in the field by the sol
diers, which, however, will not detract
from the interest of the match. Three
of Stanford's best men are laid on the
shelf â Dole, Wilson and Burmeister, the
latter receiving his injuries in the prac
tice game on Monday evening.

phine Walter for a partial distribution
of the estate of her late husband, Gus
tav Walter. Mrs. Walter also asks that
$20,000 In cash out of the distributive
Bhare, to which she is entitled, be paid
her.

and all this frontage at the end of streets
and between them is now adjudged to be

front of bun Francisco, where the State
Is exercising full power.
"Oakland's real water frontage, where
vessels land, is therefore under the abso
lute control of the State and within the
easy reach of the city by projecting Its
streets wherever necessary ' to that ship
ping frontage.
"The condemnation damages will never
be excessive, as they must oe determined
by a Jury of the county, who would
award damages according to the actual
value of the water lots in the strip and
not according to the shipping frontage
which belongs to the State.
"The foregoing supposes the city to be
in the position or having to condemn lands
at the foot of all streets, but *ueh is not
the case. The Supreme Court's decision
decided that wherever streets were opened
to iowtide line on April 1, ISoS (the time of
the compromise) the city still had the
right â¢without compensation or condemna
tion to proceed with the use of all such
streets out to the lino of Stata control,
which is lowtide line.
"There are many such streets," con
tinued Mr. Davis, "and upon the present
retrial of the cause testimony has been
taken for several weeks showing the stat
us and use of all the streets ending at
the estuary and those ending at West
Oakland, on April 1, 1868. The evidence
as to these thoroughfares Is voluminous
and will probably occupy the court an
other month.
"The exact situation, in a few words, I
is this: The Supreme Court awards to
the Water Front Company under the
compromise of I*6S:
"i- irstâ Nothing in tho basin cast of
that branch of the estuary which turns
up into Lake Merritt.
"Secondâ In the bed of the es
tuary of San Antonio except the narrow
strip on its north shore lying between,
ordinary high and low tide.
"Thirdâ Nothing In front of the city of '
Alameda.
"Fourthâ Nothing In the bay westward
from low tide line off West Oakland.
"Fifthâ The water lots or shoals off |
West Oakland to low tide line.
"Sixthâ The Supreme Court of the Unit- I
ed States awards to the public of the !
State of California all the navigable
waters In the basin, In tho estuary, in,
front of Alameda and In front of Oak
land waterward from low tide line. This
gives public control of every wharf, dock,
Blip or pier ever to be used In developing <
the commerce of the east side of the bay
and the city of Oakland.
"So there is plenty of room for build- '
Ing of ferries and all the docking facili
ties required for a great city.
"The hoodoo number 13 evidently had '
something to do with the settlement of
this famous case by which the Btate re- i
covered its property and the city of Oak- I
land gained access to the commerce of i
the Pacific," Bald Mr. Davis, with a ,
laugh. "Carpentler's famous grab of thÂ»
water frontage was made thirteen day.s !
after the incorporation of tho town of i
Oakland. In trying to maintain his hold- !
Ings of the property there were thirteen.
Buits, each of which invalidated the ef
fects of the preceding action. During the
final trial we put in evidence 13 000 000
people annually passed over this water

PRACTICALLY ASSURED
HARBOR COMMISSIONERS TTRA-R
RECOMMENDATIONS.
The Naval Beserve Vessel Marlon Is
to Be Moved Into the Stream
Some Time To-Morrow.
The Harbor Commissioners met yester
day afternoon and decided to order the
naval reserve steamer Marion into the
stream. Assistant Chief Wharfinger
Scott served the notice last night, and the
warship will be moved some time to-mor
row.
Major Harney made a voluminous re
port on the free market. He recommends
the setting aside of the wharves at the
foot of Market, Clay, Washington and
Jackson streets and the bulkheads. A
superintendent and all the necessary em
ployes are to be appointed, and the mar
ket Is to be in operation next spring.
Commissioner Herrold made a report on
the fire boats and hydrants. He recom
mends that five hydrants Instead of two
be put, and that a fire drill for all the
employes of the new ferry. depot be held
once a month.
Thrown From a Wagon.
Henry Winn, a glasscutter living at 267
Brannan street, was taken to the Receiv
ing Hospital yesterday afternoon suf
fering from concussion of the brain. He
was in a wagon and the horses bolted.
At Fourteenth and Mission streets Winn
was thrown out and alighted on his head.
His condition is serious.
Alice Johnson Defeated.
Alice Johnson, the soubrette who suSd
the Gustav Walter Orpheum Company to
recover several weeks' salary, met defeat
in Judge Cook's court yesterday. The
court held that In discharging her as in
competent the Orpheum Company exer
cised a lawful authority.

MUST CLAIM THE PRISONER.
JAMES FLOOD Will Be Released Unless Japan
Demands His Extradition.
IT IS expected that at 9 oclock this morning, James Flood, the mer
chant of Kobe, Japan, who was arrested on the Doric, on her arrival
here last Monday, by the United States Marshal, will be released by
the United States Court on habeas corpus proceedings, unless the
Japanese Government demands his extradition. His attorney, Thomas D.
Riordan, has prepared the necessary papers and will file them on the
opening of the Clerk's office this morning. The arrest was made on the
rather doubtful authority of a cablegram from American Consul Lyons
at Kobe, the charge alleged being "fraud." Marshal Shine was advised
by the State Department at Washington yesterday that Flood could not
be held legally on the authority of an American Consul in Japan, and
that he should be released from custody unless the Japanese Govern
ment requested his detention. The Marshal has been waiting three days
for some word from the Japanese Government, but up to yesterday
afternoon had received no orders. After a consultation between Mar
shal Shine, the prisoner and Mr. Riordan, it was decided to return Mr.
Flood to the Alameda Jail until this morning, in order to allow the Jap
anese Government an opportunity of taking somo action should it so de-
Blre. If the expected communication should not arrive, the defendant
will be brought into court and discharged from custody.
Flood was taken from the Alameda Jail yesterday afternoon and
spent three hours in the Appraiser's building, in the office of Marshal
Shine. He refused to make any statement, beyond the assertion that he
had been guilty of no offense and that he expected to secure his liberty
in a short time.
Mr. Riordan said that the firm of which his client was a member had
failed through mismanagement, but there was nothing criminal in the
matter. It was learned from another source that one of the chief causes
of the failure was the refusal of several Japanese subcontractors to car
ry out their contracts with the firm.
Flood has been in the carpet and matting business in Japan for five
years. He visited San Francisco last July. When he left Japan there
was no charge against him and he left openly and under his true name.
He is a son of Henry S. Flood of this city. His relatives are well-to-do.

"We entered the establishment at 11

ered Into a vat of hot water to loosen the

If the offense is repeated I will take steps

"This concern has been handling eigh
teen tons of vegetables a day, putting
the same into cans fur various reputable
grocers. The cans do not bear the came
of the canning company, but the individ
ual brands of the customers. These to
matoes are not for cheap lodging-houses,
but for the tables of the people who buy
the best they can get in the way 01 pro
visions. I am determined to put a stop
to such doings in this city. The proprie
tors of the canning works begged the
privilege of selling the 400 cans that bad
been already put up, but I seized the
whole lot."
Will Lecture on California.
Mrs. Janet Macdonald will depart for
New York shortly. While there she will
depict, by word of mouth and by picture,
the places of interest in this State, its in
dustries, its resources and its scenery.
The lecturer, who is well known on the
coast, has received an honorary appoint
ment from Governor Budd to lecture in
the East on California. She is thoroughly
acquainted with every subject bearing on
the State and has received the indorse
ment of the State Board of Trade and of
the San Francisco Chamber of Commerce.
She will make her residence at 15 West
Twenty-fourth street, New York.
ADDS A NEW
STEAMER TO
ITS FLEET
Launch of the Ferry-
Boat Berkeley.
IT WAS A GREAT SUCCESS
MISS BTJBY RICHARDS DID THE
CHRISTENING.
Berkeley's Town Trustees Presented
the Vessel With a Hand-
Borne New Set o*
Flags.
The Southern Pacific has at last added
a new steamer to Its fleet of ferry-boats.
It took the corporation a long time to
find out that a n*w boat was required,
but when it did come to the knowledge it
spared no expense on the new vessel. In
consequence the steel ferry-boat Berke
ley that was launched from the Union
Iron Works yesterday was a credit aliko
to builders and owner?.
To show how long the Southern Pacific
has been making up its mind it Is only
necessary to state that the Piedmont, the
youngest of the fleet, wont into commis
sion fif't't-n years ago, and nearly all the
others are old enough to vote. The Ala
meda wm built in 1566, the El Capltan in
IS6B. Oakland in 1575, Newark in 1877, Bay
City in IS7B, Garden City in 1879 and Pied
mont in ISB3.
The launch of the Berkeley took place
at noon yesterday. There were a large
number of invited guests at the Union Iron
Works, and the seawall as far as the
Arctic Oil Works was crowded with peo
ple. The tugboats Rescue, Sea Queen and
Governor Markham were present with
parties of excursionists, and rowboats
and yachts were there in numbers.
The Town Trustees of Berkeley were
present In a body and the new vessel
was christened by Mlgs Ruby Richards,
daughter of the president of the board.
The set of flags presented by the town of
Berkeley were llown from the steamer' .-4
flagpoles. Other decorations were flown
ana the Berkeley presented a very pretty
Appearance a.^ she glidt-d gracefully into
the wtuer. Steam was up in her boilers
and she could have gone into commission
right away had the contractors so de
sired. She will have her trial trip on the
bay in a few days.
The Berkeley has seating capacity for
over 1700 people. On the hurricane deck
1135 people can be accommodated, while
the lower deck will seat 600 more. This {3
as against 1100 or 1200. the seating capaci
ty of the old boats. The entire space on
the main deck is taken up with seats for
passengers, except that portion occupied
by the engine hatches, the stairway to
the lower hold and the two gangways for
vehicles and trucks. The hold of the ves
pel contains the machinery, boilers, quar
ters for the crew, coal bunkers, barroom
and restaurant. The Berkeley is expect
ed to be the fastest boat on the bay, but
Captain Mackenzie says she will have to
travel to beat the San Rafael.
FROM SEA AND SHORE.
The River Steamer F. M. Smith Has
at Last Got Out of Alviso Creek.
The steamer City of Panama got away
for Central American ports yesterday.
Captain Crowell took his Bhotgun along
and Intends keeping a sharp lookout for
the Examiner's pirate. The Umatilla
sailed for Puget Sound ports and the Car
acas for Mexican ports. All three ves
sels took away large cargoes but not a
great many passengers.
The stern wheel steamer F. M. Smith
got out of her bed of mud In Alvlso
Creek and was towed to "Washington
street wharf by the tug Liberty. She
was not damaged a great deal, and will
be In commission again In a few days.
The trade to Suisun by water has
grown wonderfully, and the gasoline
sloop Alta Is now too small for it. "ies
terduy she was hauled off and the
Bchooner Jennie Griffin put on in her
place
The steamer Mary Ellen Galvln has
been moved again. Yesterday she was
moved around to Powell street wharf to
make room for the Aztec. The latter ves
sel is to have new boilers put in and Is
to receive a thorough overhauling.
Captain H. Smith left for Tacoma last
night to take command of the mail steam
er Tacoma. Captain Smith was for
merly master of a British sailing ves
sel Then he was master of the collier
San Benito, and was in charge of her
when she was wrecked near Point Arena.
Latterly he has been mate of the steamer
Bristol. He has a host of friends in San
Francisco, and they are all delighted to
hear of his promotion.
The schooner Abbie M. Deering arived
from Victoria, B. C.Â« yesterday to fit out
for a sealing and otter hunting cruise.
The captain reports plenty of seal in Ber
ing Sea, but the weather too rough to
hunt them.
Mrs. Barry Not Poisoned.
Morgue Chemist Morgan yesterday
completed his chemical analysis of the
stomach of Mrs. Richard Barry, who died
of hemorrhage of the lungs in a lodging
house on Anthony street last week. He
found some morphine, but not enough to
have caused death, and will so report at
the Inquest this morning.

The fight began when Governor Lord

S(*iator in default of an election by the

ber 26. Corbett had a large following, out

was nominated. One ballot made him the

at the session of the Legislature next day

"It was extremely gratifving 1 to me to
have the election come as v did." said the
victor of this long fight yesterday. "I
received sixty-four out of sixty-six Re
publican votesâ the sixty-fifth being my
own vote, and the sixty-sixth that of an

"Mr. Corbett and I have been friends
always and I would not run while he was
in the field, but when he withdrew and
they asked me I could not refuse. It was
the Federal influence wielded by McÃride
which kept Corbett out.
"The outlook for the Republican party
In Oregon never was better. It Is stand
ing solid and will do so for twenty years
to come. Populism Is on its last legs up
there and I do not think it will cut much
of a figure in the future political history
of the State.
"My election is really a triumph for the
gold "standard. It has been an issue right
along in Oregon politics, and I have al
ways been known as an out and out gold
man. The financial^ question has been
mixed up In all the recent campaigns, but
the silver men have met with so many ae
feats that I think the question is prac
tically settled so far as we are concerned.
Oregon is a gold standard State."
Simon has for years been a prominent
figure in Oregon politics. He was born
In Germany In 1851, and went to Oregon
with his parents In 1557. He was educated
In the public schools of Portland, studied
law and in 1572 was admitted to the bar.
since which time he has practiced hlÂ«
profession.
He became a member of the City Coun
cil in 1577, serving three years, being for
a portion of the time president of the
Council and acting Mayor of the city.
As secretary of the Republican State Cen
tral Committee In ISTB he managed the
campaign so successfully that in ISBO he
was made chairman of the committee,
in which capacity he served for six yearn.
He represented Multnomah County In
the State Senate from ISBO to 1891, and
again since in 1893.
He was president of the Senate dur
ing the session of 1889-91 and since 1893
has been repeatedly re-elected to that
fiosltlon, at present acting In that capac
ty He is one of the best presiding offi
cers that body ever had. In 1892 Mr.
Simon was a delegate to the National
Republican Convention in Minneapolis.
He was then elected to represent Ore
gon In the Republican committee. For
eight years Mr. Simon was a member of
the Board of Police Commissioners of
Portland. He was a law partner of the
late Senator Dolph, being a member of
the firm of Dolph, Bellinger, Mallory &
in his trip to this city Senator Simon
Is acompanled by George A. Steel, chair
man of the Republican State Central
